Checkpoint
What is Ibn Taymiyya's main criticism of the logicians' distinction between awwaliyat and mashhurat?
A) He denies the existence of any self-evident propositions.
B) He argues the distinction is based on a false ontological claim about middle terms in reality.
C) He agrees that the distinction is based on objective reality.
D) He claims that all propositions are equally self-evident.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya reduces the logicians' objective classification to a relative epistemological judgment, undermining the fixed division between first principles and common notions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique to a specific example: In a debate about whether the existence of the Creator is self-evident (badihi) or requires proof, use his argument to show that this distinction is relative to the listener's knowledge and background.
?
Identify a proposition that is widely accepted as self-evident in one Islamic madhab but requires proof in another. Explain using Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological argument.

The Relativity of Self-Evidence
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the intrinsic self-evidence of propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument that self-evidence (bayyin) is relational rather than intrinsic, and distinguish it from the logicians' claim that self-evidence is an inherent property of propositions.

Core Idea
Clarity is not in the proposition but in the knower.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational assumption of the logicians: that some propositions are intrinsically self-evident (bayyin) while others are not. He argues that what is clear to one person may be obscure to another, because clarity depends on the knower's knowledge and mental capacity. This has profound implications for how we evaluate arguments and define 'primary' (awwali) propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the logicians' division of necessary attributes into what is self-evident and what requires evidence. Note that they treat this division as intrinsic to the attributes.
The logicians claim some properties are known without a middle term in reality.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-argument
Pinpoint his statement that 'self-evident' (bayyin) is not a description of the thing itself, but of man's knowledge of it. He says it is 'an informing about mental existence, not external existence.'
This is a shift from ontological to epistemological.
3
Analyze the relational nature
Notice that he calls it 'a relation and addition between the known and the knowing man.' Then he gives the example of Zayd and 'Amr to illustrate variation.
Differences in knowledge, perception, and intellect cause variation.
4
Apply the critique to 'primary' propositions
He then applies this to the concept of 'awwali (primary) propositions, which logicians define as those requiring no middle term. He argues that 'primary' is also relative, not a fixed genus.
The distinction is correct only as a relative one, not as a classification of proposition types.

ولا ريب أن الصفات اللازمة للموصوف تنقسم إلى ما لزومه بين للإنسان، وإلى ما ليس هو بيناً بل يفتقر ملزومه إلى دليل. وكونه بيناً للإنسان وغير بين ليس هو الشيء في نفسه، وإنما هو إخبار عن علم الإنسان به وتنبيه له. فهو إخبار عن الوجود الذهني، لا الخارجي فما كان بيناً للإنسان معلوماً له موجوداً في ذهنه لم يحتج فيه إلى دليل . وما لم يكن كذلك احتاج فيه إلى دليل وكون الشيء بيناً وغير بين نسبة وإضافة بين المعلوم والإنسان العالم. وهذا تختلف فيه أحوال الناس، فقد يتبين لزيد ما لا يتبين لعمرو . فإن أسباب العلم وقوة الشعور وجودة الأذهان متفاوتة. فلا يلزم إذا تبين لإنسان ثبوتُ بعض الصفات أو لزومها أو اتصاف الموصوف بها أن يتبين ذلك لكل أحد، ولا يلزم إذا خفي على بعض الناس أن يكون خفياً على كل أحد . وحينئذ، فإذا فرق بين الأولي وغيره بأن الأولي ما لا يحتاج إلى وسط، فبين القضية أولية أو غير أولية كون القضية أولية أو غير أولية فرق إضافي بحسب أحوال الناس . فيكون ذلك بمنزلة أن يقال : القضية إما أن تكون معلومة وإما أن لا تكون وإما أن تكون ظاهرة وإما أن لا تكون، وإما أن تكون واضحة وإما أن لا تكون وإما أن تكون جلية وإما أن لا تكون وإما أن يتصور الإنسان صدقها وإما أن لا يتصور. وهذا فرق صحيح، فإن كل قضية بالنسبة إلى كل أحد إما أن تكون بينه له وإما أن لا تكون بينة له . لكن ليس هذا الفرق يميز بين أجناس القضايا حتى يجعل جنس منها يعود بتمامه من قبيل غير البين وجنس آخر بتمامه من جنس البين . فإن هذا الفرق لا إلى صفة لازمة للقضية كما ادعاه من أن اللازم البين ما ثبت للموصوف بلا وسط في نفس الأمر، واللازم غير البين ما كان ثبوته بوسط في نفس الأمر. بل جميع اللوازم للموصوف ليس بينها وبين الموصوف وسط أصلاً في نفس الأمر. وإن احتاج الإنسان في علمه بثبوتها إلى دليل فليس كل ما لا يعلم لزومه للموصوف إلا بدليل لا يكون ثبوته له إلا بوسط، كما تقدم بيانه. فإن المعلوم لازم لعلته المعينة، لا يمكن تخلف المعلول عن علته التامة التي دخل فيها وجود الشروط وانتفاء الموانع . ومع هذا فقد لا يعلم ثبوت المعلول إلا بوسط بأن يستدل بمعلول آخر على العلة، ثم بالعلة على المعلول. كما يستدل بالرائحة التي في الشراب على أنه مسكر، ويستدل بثبوت المسكر على التحريم ، كقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : كل مسكر خمر، وكل مسكر حرام - رواه مسلم. وكما يستدل بثبوت كفّارة اليمين في الالتزام على أنه يمين، ويستدل بأنه يمين على انعقاد الإيلاء به وكذلك بالعكس. وكما يستدل بالشعاع المنتشر على طلوع الشمس، وبطلوعها على فوات الفجر وكما يستدل بفيء الظلال على زوال الشمس وبزوالها على دخول وقت الظهر. فإنها لا تفيء من المغرب إلى المشرق إلا إذا زالت الشمس، ولا يسمى فيئاً إلا ما كان بعد الزوال، و الظل اسم عام لما كان في أول النهار وآخره. وإن شئت تستدل بتزايد الظلال بعد تناهي قِصَرها على الزوال، وبه على دخول وقت الظهر. وكما يستدل بالجدي على جهة الشمال، ويستدل بها على جهة الكعبة بأن يعلم أن الكعبة في تلك الأرض من جهة الجنوب والقطب شماليها، إما محاذياً للركن الشامي سواء كما في أرض حَرَّان وما سامتها، وإما مائلاً إلى المشرق وجهة الباب فينحرف إلى المغرب، كما في أرض العراق. ومثل هذا كثير . وبالجملة، فـ الدليل باتفاق العقلاء أعم من العلة ، بل كل ملزوم يستلزم غيره يمكن الاستدلال به عليه مطلقاً ولا يفرق في هذا بين لازم ولازم، ولا يقال: من اللوازم ما يلزم بوسط، فلا يمكن العلم به إلا بعد ذلك الوسط، بل يمكن الاستدلال به عليه كما يمكن الاستدلال بكل ملزوم على لازمه. فإن شرط ذلك العلم بالملزوم، متى علم أن هذا لازم لهذا استدل بالملزوم على اللازم. فقد يكون ما هو لازم بغير وسط عندهم ولا يعرف لزومه إلا بالوسط الذي هو الدليل، كالمعلول مع العلة. وقد يكون ما يزعمون أنه لازم بوسط ويمكن العلم بلزومه بلا وسط . كما ذكروه من المثال في ذلك، فزعموا أن اللونية للسواد و الجسمية للحيوان ذاتية لثبوتها بلا وسط وزعموا أن الزوجية والفردية للعدد عَرَضية. قالوا : لأنا وإن علمنا أن الأربعة زوج ، و الثلاثة فرد فلا يعلم ذلك في كل الأعداد، بل
And there is no doubt that the necessary attributes of the described are divided into what is self-evident in its necessity for man, and what is not self-evident but rather its necessitating requires evidence. And its being self-evident for man or not self-evident is not the thing itself, but rather it is an informing about man's knowledge of it and a reminder for him. So it is an informing about mental existence, not external existence. So what was self-evident for man, known to him, existing in his mind, did not need evidence. And what was not like that needed evidence. And the thing being self-evident or not is a relation and addition between the known and the knowing man. And people's conditions vary in this, so what becomes clear to Zayd may not become clear to 'Amr. For the causes of knowledge, strength of perception, and quality of minds are different. So it does not follow that if the proving of some attributes or their necessity or the described being qualified with them becomes clear to a person, it must become clear to everyone; nor does it follow that if it is hidden from some people, it must be hidden from everyone. Then, when he distinguished between the primary and other than it by saying the primary is what does not need a middle term, then the proposition being primary or non-primary is an additional distinction according to people's conditions. So that is like saying: the proposition is either known or not known, or apparent or not apparent, or clear or not clear, or evident or not evident, or man perceives its truth or does not perceive it. And this distinction is correct, for every proposition relative to every individual is either self-evident for him or not self-evident for him. But this distinction does not differentiate between genera of propositions such that one genus is entirely of the non-self-evident class and another genus is entirely of the self-evident class. For this distinction does not pertain to a necessary attribute of the proposition, as he claimed that the self-evident necessary property is what is proven for the described without a middle term in reality, and the non-self-evident necessary property is what its proving is by a middle term in reality. Rather, all necessary properties of the described have no middle term between them and the described at all in reality. And if man needs in his knowledge of their proving to evidence, not everything whose necessity for the described is not known except by evidence is such that its proving for it is only by a middle term, as was clarified previously. For the effect is necessary for its specific cause; the effect cannot lag behind its complete cause which includes the presence of conditions and absence of impediments. And despite this, the proving of the effect may not be known except by a middle term, by using another effect as evidence for the cause, then the cause as evidence for the effect. As one uses the smell in the drink as evidence that it is intoxicating, and uses the proving of intoxication as evidence for prohibition, like his saying peace be upon him: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every intoxicant is forbidden' - narrated by Muslim. And as one uses the proving of the expiation for an oath in the commitment as evidence that it is an oath, and uses the fact that it is an oath as evidence for the validity of declaring a vow of abstinence (ila') by it, and likewise conversely. And as one uses the spreading rays as evidence for sunrise, and the sunrise as evidence for the passing of dawn. And as one uses the shadow's length decrease as evidence for the sun's declination, and its declination as evidence for the entry of the noon prayer time. For shadows do not lengthen from west to east except when the sun has passed the meridian, and it is not called 'fayy' except what is after declination, and 'zill' is a general name for what is at the beginning and end of the day. And if you wish, you use the increase of shadows after their shortness reaches its limit as evidence for declination, and it as evidence for the entry of noon time. And as one uses the North Star as evidence for the direction of the north, and uses it as evidence for the direction of the Ka'ba by knowing that the Ka'ba in that land is towards the south and the pole is north of it, either aligned with the Syrian corner equally as in the land of Harran and its parallels, or inclined to the east and the direction of the door so one deviates to the west as in the land of Iraq. And such examples are many. In summary, 'evidence' by agreement of rational people is more general than cause; rather, every necessitating thing that necessitates another can be used as evidence for it absolutely, and no distinction is made in this between one necessary property and another. And it is not said: 'Among necessary properties are those that are necessary through a middle term, so knowledge of them cannot be attained except after that middle term.' Rather, it can be used as evidence for it just as every necessitating thing can be used as evidence for its necessary consequence. For the condition of that is knowledge of the necessitating thing; whenever it is known that this is necessary for this, one uses the necessitating as evidence for the necessary. So sometimes what is necessary without a middle term according to them is not known to be necessary except through the middle term which is the evidence, like the effect with the cause. And sometimes what they claim is necessary through a middle term can be known to be necessary without a middle term. As they mentioned as an example in that, they claimed that 'color-ness' for blackness and 'body-ness' for animal are essential due to their being proven without a middle term, and they claimed that evenness and oddness for number are accidental. They said: 'For even if we know that four is even and three is odd, that is not known in all numbers, but...'
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' classification of necessary properties into those that are self-evident (bayyin) and those that are not, as well as against the idea that some properties are proven with a middle term (wasat) in reality. Ibn Taymiyya begins by establishing that the clarity or obscurity of a proposition is not an intrinsic property but a relation to the knower. He states that necessary attributes of a subject divide into those whose necessity is self-evident to a person and those that require evidence. However, being self-evident is not a statement about the thing itself but about human knowledge—it refers to mental existence, not external. Therefore, what is self-evident to one person may not be to another, due to differences in reasoning ability and background. This leads to the conclusion that if something is clear to Zayd, it does not mean it must be clear to everyone, and if hidden from some, it may still be clear to others. He then addresses the logicians' concept of 'primary' (awwali) propositions, which they define as those not needing a middle term. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this distinction is merely relative and does not partition propositions into kinds. In reality, all necessary attributes are proven without a middle term; the middle term only exists in the mind of the knower who needs evidence to perceive the connection. He provides the example of a cause and its effect: the effect is necessarily dependent on its complete cause, yet sometimes we only know the effect through a middle term (another effect), or we use the effect to infer the cause, and then the cause to infer other effects. He then gives a series of concrete examples from Islamic law and natural phenomena: using the smell of a drink to infer it is intoxicating, then the intoxicating property to infer prohibition (based on the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr, every intoxicant is forbidden'); using the existence of expiation for an oath to infer it is an oath, and the oath to infer the validity of ila'; using spreading rays to infer sunrise, and sunrise to infer the end of dawn; using the lengthening of shadows after their shortest point to infer the sun's declination (zawal), and the declination to infer the entry of noon prayer time; using the North Star to find north, and then north to find the direction of the Ka'ba, with variations for different lands. He concludes that 'evidence' (dalil) is more general than 'cause' ('illa); any necessitating thing can be used as evidence for its necessary consequence, regardless of whether it is considered essential or accidental. Thus, the logicians' attempt to distinguish between properties that are proven 'without a middle term' and those 'with a middle term' is unfounded, because the middle term is only in the knower's mind, not in reality. He ends by criticizing their specific example that 'colorness' for blackness is essential (known without a middle term) while evenness for numbers is accidental (known only through a middle term), pointing out that we may know evenness in individual numbers without a middle term, but they claim it is not known for all numbers—thus the distinction is flawed.

أولي
وسط
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what is the correct understanding of 'self-evident' (bayyin)?
A) A property inherent in certain propositions, independent of the knower.
B) A relational property between the known and the knowing person, varying with people's conditions.
C) A synonym for 'primary' (awwali) propositions that do not need a middle term in reality.
D) A term that applies only to essential attributes like 'colorness' for blackness.

Key Takeaway
Self-evidence is relative to the knower and does not create fixed genera of propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Evidence is Broader than Cause
Ibn Taymiyya's argument that evidence is broader than cause, using examples from fiqh and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's assertion that evidence (dalil) is more general than cause ('illa), using examples from fiqh and natural phenomena, and critique the logicians' claim that some necessary properties require a middle term in reality.

Core Idea
Any necessitating thing can serve as evidence, regardless of direction.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique by addressing the logicians' claim that some necessary properties are proven through a middle term (wasat) in reality. He argues that in the external world, all necessary properties are equally connected without a middle term; the middle term only exists in human reasoning. He then provides a series of examples from Islamic law and nature to demonstrate that evidence (dalil) can be used in both directions—from cause to effect and from effect to cause—establishing that dalil is broader than 'illa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' ontological claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that logicians claim some necessary properties are proven 'without a middle term in reality' and others 'with a middle term in reality.' This is the target of his critique.
The logicians treat the middle term as part of the ontological structure.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
He asserts that all necessary properties have no middle term in reality between them and the subject. The middle term only appears in the knower's mind when they need evidence to perceive the connection.
This is a crucial move from ontology to epistemology.
3
Analyze the examples from fiqh
Examine the example of intoxicating drink: smell (effect) evidences intoxication (cause), and intoxication (cause) evidences prohibition (effect). Note the bidirectional use of evidence.
The same relationship can be used in both directions, showing evidence is broader.
4
Examine the natural examples
Study the examples of sunlight, shadows, and the North Star. See how each pair of necessitating and necessary can swap roles as evidence.
Evidence is not restricted to cause->effect; effect->cause is equally valid.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that evidence (dalil) is, by agreement of rational people, more general than cause. Any necessitating thing can be evidence for its necessary consequence, and vice versa.
This undermines the logicians' attempt to classify properties by middle term.

ولا ريب أن الصفات اللازمة للموصوف تنقسم إلى ما لزومه بين للإنسان، وإلى ما ليس هو بيناً بل يفتقر ملزومه إلى دليل. وكونه بيناً للإنسان وغير بين ليس هو الشيء في نفسه، وإنما هو إخبار عن علم الإنسان به وتنبيه له. فهو إخبار عن الوجود الذهني، لا الخارجي فما كان بيناً للإنسان معلوماً له موجوداً في ذهنه لم يحتج فيه إلى دليل . وما لم يكن كذلك احتاج فيه إلى دليل وكون الشيء بيناً وغير بين نسبة وإضافة بين المعلوم والإنسان العالم. وهذا تختلف فيه أحوال الناس، فقد يتبين لزيد ما لا يتبين لعمرو . فإن أسباب العلم وقوة الشعور وجودة الأذهان متفاوتة. فلا يلزم إذا تبين لإنسان ثبوتُ بعض الصفات أو لزومها أو اتصاف الموصوف بها أن يتبين ذلك لكل أحد، ولا يلزم إذا خفي على بعض الناس أن يكون خفياً على كل أحد . وحينئذ، فإذا فرق بين الأولي وغيره بأن الأولي ما لا يحتاج إلى وسط، فبين القضية أولية أو غير أولية كون القضية أولية أو غير أولية فرق إضافي بحسب أحوال الناس . فيكون ذلك بمنزلة أن يقال : القضية إما أن تكون معلومة وإما أن لا تكون وإما أن تكون ظاهرة وإما أن لا تكون، وإما أن تكون واضحة وإما أن لا تكون وإما أن تكون جلية وإما أن لا تكون وإما أن يتصور الإنسان صدقها وإما أن لا يتصور. وهذا فرق صحيح، فإن كل قضية بالنسبة إلى كل أحد إما أن تكون بينه له وإما أن لا تكون بينة له . لكن ليس هذا الفرق يميز بين أجناس القضايا حتى يجعل جنس منها يعود بتمامه من قبيل غير البين وجنس آخر بتمامه من جنس البين . فإن هذا الفرق لا إلى صفة لازمة للقضية كما ادعاه من أن اللازم البين ما ثبت للموصوف بلا وسط في نفس الأمر، واللازم غير البين ما كان ثبوته بوسط في نفس الأمر. بل جميع اللوازم للموصوف ليس بينها وبين الموصوف وسط أصلاً في نفس الأمر. وإن احتاج الإنسان في علمه بثبوتها إلى دليل فليس كل ما لا يعلم لزومه للموصوف إلا بدليل لا يكون ثبوته له إلا بوسط، كما تقدم بيانه. فإن المعلوم لازم لعلته المعينة، لا يمكن تخلف المعلول عن علته التامة التي دخل فيها وجود الشروط وانتفاء الموانع . ومع هذا فقد لا يعلم ثبوت المعلول إلا بوسط بأن يستدل بمعلول آخر على العلة، ثم بالعلة على المعلول. كما يستدل بالرائحة التي في الشراب على أنه مسكر، ويستدل بثبوت المسكر على التحريم ، كقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : كل مسكر خمر، وكل مسكر حرام - رواه مسلم. وكما يستدل بثبوت كفّارة اليمين في الالتزام على أنه يمين، ويستدل بأنه يمين على انعقاد الإيلاء به وكذلك بالعكس. وكما يستدل بالشعاع المنتشر على طلوع الشمس، وبطلوعها على فوات الفجر وكما يستدل بفيء الظلال على زوال الشمس وبزوالها على دخول وقت الظهر. فإنها لا تفيء من المغرب إلى المشرق إلا إذا زالت الشمس، ولا يسمى فيئاً إلا ما كان بعد الزوال، و الظل اسم عام لما كان في أول النهار وآخره. وإن شئت تستدل بتزايد الظلال بعد تناهي قِصَرها على الزوال، وبه على دخول وقت الظهر. وكما يستدل بالجدي على جهة الشمال، ويستدل بها على جهة الكعبة بأن يعلم أن الكعبة في تلك الأرض من جهة الجنوب والقطب شماليها، إما محاذياً للركن الشامي سواء كما في أرض حَرَّان وما سامتها، وإما مائلاً إلى المشرق وجهة الباب فينحرف إلى المغرب، كما في أرض العراق. ومثل هذا كثير . وبالجملة، فـ الدليل باتفاق العقلاء أعم من العلة ، بل كل ملزوم يستلزم غيره يمكن الاستدلال به عليه مطلقاً ولا يفرق في هذا بين لازم ولازم، ولا يقال: من اللوازم ما يلزم بوسط، فلا يمكن العلم به إلا بعد ذلك الوسط، بل يمكن الاستدلال به عليه كما يمكن الاستدلال بكل ملزوم على لازمه. فإن شرط ذلك العلم بالملزوم، متى علم أن هذا لازم لهذا استدل بالملزوم على اللازم. فقد يكون ما هو لازم بغير وسط عندهم ولا يعرف لزومه إلا بالوسط الذي هو الدليل، كالمعلول مع العلة. وقد يكون ما يزعمون أنه لازم بوسط ويمكن العلم بلزومه بلا وسط . كما ذكروه من المثال في ذلك، فزعموا أن اللونية للسواد و الجسمية للحيوان ذاتية لثبوتها بلا وسط وزعموا أن الزوجية والفردية للعدد عَرَضية. قالوا : لأنا وإن علمنا أن الأربعة زوج ، و الثلاثة فرد فلا يعلم ذلك في كل الأعداد، بل
And there is no doubt that the necessary attributes of the described are divided into what is self-evident in its necessity for man, and what is not self-evident but rather its necessitating requires evidence. And its being self-evident for man or not self-evident is not the thing itself, but rather it is an informing about man's knowledge of it and a reminder for him. So it is an informing about mental existence, not external existence. So what was self-evident for man, known to him, existing in his mind, did not need evidence. And what was not like that needed evidence. And the thing being self-evident or not is a relation and addition between the known and the knowing man. And people's conditions vary in this, so what becomes clear to Zayd may not become clear to 'Amr. For the causes of knowledge, strength of perception, and quality of minds are different. So it does not follow that if the proving of some attributes or their necessity or the described being qualified with them becomes clear to a person, it must become clear to everyone; nor does it follow that if it is hidden from some people, it must be hidden from everyone. Then, when he distinguished between the primary and other than it by saying the primary is what does not need a middle term, then the proposition being primary or non-primary is an additional distinction according to people's conditions. So that is like saying: the proposition is either known or not known, or apparent or not apparent, or clear or not clear, or evident or not evident, or man perceives its truth or does not perceive it. And this distinction is correct, for every proposition relative to every individual is either self-evident for him or not self-evident for him. But this distinction does not differentiate between genera of propositions such that one genus is entirely of the non-self-evident class and another genus is entirely of the self-evident class. For this distinction does not pertain to a necessary attribute of the proposition, as he claimed that the self-evident necessary property is what is proven for the described without a middle term in reality, and the non-self-evident necessary property is what its proving is by a middle term in reality. Rather, all necessary properties of the described have no middle term between them and the described at all in reality. And if man needs in his knowledge of their proving to evidence, not everything whose necessity for the described is not known except by evidence is such that its proving for it is only by a middle term, as was clarified previously. For the effect is necessary for its specific cause; the effect cannot lag behind its complete cause which includes the presence of conditions and absence of impediments. And despite this, the proving of the effect may not be known except by a middle term, by using another effect as evidence for the cause, then the cause as evidence for the effect. As one uses the smell in the drink as evidence that it is intoxicating, and uses the proving of intoxication as evidence for prohibition, like his saying peace be upon him: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every intoxicant is forbidden' - narrated by Muslim. And as one uses the proving of the expiation for an oath in the commitment as evidence that it is an oath, and uses the fact that it is an oath as evidence for the validity of declaring a vow of abstinence (ila') by it, and likewise conversely. And as one uses the spreading rays as evidence for sunrise, and the sunrise as evidence for the passing of dawn. And as one uses the shadow's length decrease as evidence for the sun's declination, and its declination as evidence for the entry of the noon prayer time. For shadows do not lengthen from west to east except when the sun has passed the meridian, and it is not called 'fayy' except what is after declination, and 'zill' is a general name for what is at the beginning and end of the day. And if you wish, you use the increase of shadows after their shortness reaches its limit as evidence for declination, and it as evidence for the entry of noon time. And as one uses the North Star as evidence for the direction of the north, and uses it as evidence for the direction of the Ka'ba by knowing that the Ka'ba in that land is towards the south and the pole is north of it, either aligned with the Syrian corner equally as in the land of Harran and its parallels, or inclined to the east and the direction of the door so one deviates to the west as in the land of Iraq. And such examples are many. In summary, 'evidence' by agreement of rational people is more general than cause; rather, every necessitating thing that necessitates another can be used as evidence for it absolutely, and no distinction is made in this between one necessary property and another. And it is not said: 'Among necessary properties are those that are necessary through a middle term, so knowledge of them cannot be attained except after that middle term.' Rather, it can be used as evidence for it just as every necessitating thing can be used as evidence for its necessary consequence. For the condition of that is knowledge of the necessitating thing; whenever it is known that this is necessary for this, one uses the necessitating as evidence for the necessary. So sometimes what is necessary without a middle term according to them is not known to be necessary except through the middle term which is the evidence, like the effect with the cause. And sometimes what they claim is necessary through a middle term can be known to be necessary without a middle term. As they mentioned as an example in that, they claimed that 'color-ness' for blackness and 'body-ness' for animal are essential due to their being proven without a middle term, and they claimed that evenness and oddness for number are accidental. They said: 'For even if we know that four is even and three is odd, that is not known in all numbers, but...'

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' classification of necessary properties into those that are self-evident (bayyin) and those that are not, as well as against the idea that some properties are proven with a middle term (wasat) in reality. He then addresses the logicians' concept of 'primary' (awwali) propositions, which they define as those not needing a middle term. He then gives a series of concrete examples from Islamic law and natural phenomena: using the smell of a drink to infer it is intoxicating, then the intoxicating property to infer prohibition (based on the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr, every intoxicant is forbidden'); using the existence of expiation for an oath to infer it is an oath, and the oath to infer the validity of ila'; using spreading rays to infer sunrise, and sunrise to infer the end of dawn; using the lengthening of shadows after their shortest point to infer the sun's declination (zawal), and the declination to infer the entry of noon prayer time; using the North Star to find north, and then north to find the direction of the Ka'ba, with variations for different lands. He ends by criticizing their specific example that 'colorness' for blackness is essential (known without a middle term) while evenness for numbers is accidental (known only through a middle term), pointing out that we may know evenness in individual numbers without a middle term, but they claim it is not known for all numbers—thus the distinction is flawed.

advanced

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Essential vs. Accidental
Ibn Taymiyya argues the essential/accidental distinction is based on knowledge, not reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the real distinction between essential and accidental attributes and explain why he considers it subjective.

Core Idea
The distinction between essential and accidental attributes is in the mind, not in reality.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a core assumption of Aristotelian logic: that some attributes are essential to a thing's essence while others are merely accidental. He uses a mathematical example to show that what we call 'essential' depends on what we know, not on the thing itself. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mathematical example
Ibn Taymiyya begins with even and odd numbers: knowing that 262 is even requires a middle term (division into two equal parts). The evenness is not a real attribute in the number but known through a process.
The example illustrates that attributes like evenness depend on human knowledge.
2
Trace the analogy to dhati and lazim
He references his earlier refutation of the logicians' distinction. He claims that evenness and oddness are necessary concomitants (lazim) of number, just as numericality is a concomitant of number itself. There is no essential difference.
He blurs the line between essential and accidental by making all attributes concomitants.
3
Note the epistemic shift
Some concomitants are known to be necessary for all individuals, while others are hidden. This difference pertains to the knower, not the thing. He compares it to visible vs. invisible objects.
The distinction is thus epistemic (based on knowledge) not ontological (in reality).
4
Reject the composition of essence
Ibn Taymiyya denies that the essence (mahiyya) existing in the external world is composed of attributes. The claim that attributes are constitutive parts prior to the essence is, in his words, 'utmost corruption'.
This undermines the entire project of defining things by genus and differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفتقر علمنا بأن «المئتين واثنين وستين زوج» و «الإحدى وستون فرد» إلى وسط، وهو أن يقسم هذه، فإذا انقسمت بقسيمين متساويين علمنا أنها زوج. الفرق لا يعود وقد بينا في كلامنا على فساد قولهم في الفرق بين الذاتي واللازم أن الفرق لا يعود إلى صفة للموصوف في نفس الأمر. بل كون الزوجية والفردية لازمة للزوج الموصوف في نفس الأمر والفرد كلزوم «العددية» للعدد ولزوم «اللونية» للبياض والسواد ولزوم «الجسمية» للحيوان. ولكن بعض اللوازم بين لبعض الناس أنه لازم لجميع الأفراد، وبعضها يخفى ثبوته لبعض الأفراد. وهذا فرق يعود إلى الإنسان كما أن من الأشياء ما نراها ومن الأشياء ما لا نراها، وكذلك من الأشياء ما نعلمها ومن الأشياء ما لا نعلمها. ولا يقتضي ذلك أن يكون ما علمه من الصفات هو أجزاء للماهية تركبت منها لو جاز أن تتركب الماهية من الأوصاف وما لم يعلمه لا يكون أجزاء للماهية. فكيف إذا كان تركُبُ الماهية الموجودة في الخارج من صفات قائمة بها؟ ودعوى أن تلك الصفات أجزاء لها مقوّمة وهي سابقة عليها في غاية الفساد.
Our knowledge that 'two hundred and sixty-two is even' and 'sixty-one is odd' requires a middle term, which is to divide them; if they divide into two equal parts we know they are even. The difference does not return. And we have explained in our discussion on the corruption of their statement regarding the distinction between the essential (dhati) and the necessary concomitant (lazim) that the difference does not return to an attribute of the described in reality. Rather, evenness and oddness are necessary concomitants of the described even and odd in reality, like the necessity of 'numericality' for number, the necessity of 'coloredness' for whiteness and blackness, and the necessity of 'corporeality' for animal. However, some concomitants are clear to some people that they are necessary for all individuals, and some are hidden in their being established for some individuals. And this difference pertains to the human [subject], just as among things there are those we see and those we do not see, and similarly among things there are those we know and those we do not know. And that does not entail that what he knows of attributes are parts of the essence composed from them — if it were permissible for the essence to be composed of attributes — and what he does not know are not parts of the essence. So how [could it be] if the composition of the essence existing externally is from attributes subsisting in it? And the claim that those attributes are parts constituting it and are prior to it is utmost corruption.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' claim that there is a real distinction between essential attributes (dhati) and necessary concomitants (lazim). Ibn Taymiyya begins with a mathematical example: to know that a number is even, we need a middle term (division into two equal parts). But the difference between evenness and oddness is not a real attribute in the number itself; rather, it is a difference in our knowledge. He references his earlier discussion on the corruption of their statement about the distinction between dhati and lazim. He explains that evenness and oddness are necessary concomitants of the number, just as numericality is a necessary concomitant of number in general, or coloredness of whiteness, or corporeality of animal. However, some concomitants are known to be necessary for all individuals, while others are hidden from some people. This difference pertains to the human knower, like the difference between visible and invisible things. But this does not mean that the attributes we know are parts of the essence (mahiyya) from which it is composed, and that what we do not know are not parts. The very idea that the essence existing in the external world is composed of attributes is absurd. Therefore, the claim that those attributes are constitutive parts prior to the essence is utmost corruption.

Key Terms
الذاتي (al-dhati)
اللازم (al-lazim)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, the difference between an essential attribute (dhati) and a necessary concomitant (lazim) is based on:
A) The real nature of the thing
B) Human knowledge and ignorance
C) The logical form of the proposition
D) The will of God

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that the essential/accidental distinction is epistemic, not ontological, undermining the basis of Aristotelian definitions and encouraging a more knowledge-dependent view of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Find a modern example where a 'definition' might be biased by the definer's knowledge, similar to Ibn Taymiyya's point. Write a short paragraph showing how the definition might change if more was known.

Refuting al-Razi's Externalization of the Middle Term
Author refutes al-Razi's notion that the mental middle term can be an external middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to al-Razi's externalization of the middle term and state his alternative definition.

Core Idea
The middle term is the proof, not an external entity.

Ibn Taymiyya here directly challenges al-Razi's innovation of treating the mental middle term as an external entity. He appeals to the authority of earlier logicians and Ibn Sina to show that this is a departure from the tradition. For Ibn Taymiyya, the middle term is simply the dalil — the evidence that connects the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Razi's Claim
Locate what al-Razi asserted about the middle term. The passage says he claimed 'that the mental middle term is an external middle term.' What does 'external' imply here?
Al-Razi attempted to reify the middle term, treating it as a real object in the world, not merely a mental concept.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation
Ibn Taymiyya calls this claim 'false' (باطل). How does he support his rejection? He says he does not know any early logician saying this, nor has he seen it in Ibn Sina's works.
He relies on authoritative precedent to undermine al-Razi's innovation.
3
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's Alternative
Ibn Taymiyya states his own view: 'the middle term is the proof' (الوسط هو الدليل). What does this mean for the role of the middle term in a syllogism?
The middle term is a logical intermediary, not a physical entity. It serves as the evidence that links the premises to the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان جعل والرازي وأمثاله لما شاركوا المنطقيين في هذا الخطأ زعموا أن الوسط الذهني وسط خارجي، فهذا باطل وما علمت قدماء المنطقيين يقولونه، ولا رأيته في كلام ابن سينا وأمثاله. وإنما الذي رأيته في كلامه أن «الوسط» هو الدليل.
The falsehood of the claim of al-Razi and his ilk that when they shared with the logicians in this error claimed that the mental middle term is an external middle term — this is false, and I do not know the early logicians saying it, nor have I seen it in the speech of Ibn Sina and his ilk. Rather, what I have seen in his speech is that 'the middle term' is the proof.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of al-Razi's claim that the middle term (al-wasat) in a syllogism can be external (khariji). Ibn Taymiyya states that this is false and that he has not encountered such a claim among the early logicians or in Ibn Sina's works. He asserts that the middle term is simply the dalil (proof), i.e., the logical intermediary, not an external object. This critique targets a specific innovation by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who allegedly attempted to reify the middle term.

Key Terms
الوسط (al-wasat): The middle term in a syllogism; Ibn Taymiyya insists it is the proof (dalil), not an external entity. [Note: In standard logic, al-wasat is the term common to both premises. Ibn Taymiyya is criticizing the notion that it is a real external thing.]
الوسط الذهني (al-wasat al-dhihni): The mental middle term; al-Razi allegedly calls it external.
الدليل (al-dalil): The evidence or proof; synonymous with the middle term for Ibn Taymiyya. [Note: In logic, dalil often means sign or indicator; here it is equated with the middle term.]

Key Takeaway
For Ibn Taymiyya, the middle term is not an external object but the proof (dalil) that links the premises – a conceptual intermediary.

Al-Razi's Proof and Refutation: Knowledge of Essence and Its Concomitants
Al-Razi's proof and its refutation through infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to deconstruct al-Razi's argument that knowledge of essence entails knowledge of its concomitant, identify the objection's infinite regress, and explain al-Razi's response concerning the relational nature of causality.

Core Idea
Knowledge of an essence does not automatically yield all its remote concomitants; causality is relational.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's critique presents al-Razi's attempt to prove that knowing an essence gives you knowledge of its necessary concomitant. An objection shows this leads to an infinite regress, forcing al-Razi to refine his position. Understanding this exchange sharpens your ability to evaluate claims about necessary entailment in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Razi's core claim
Al-Razi asserts that conceiving an essence as it is necessitates conceiving its necessary concomitant. Locate this claim in the Arabic: 'فإذا عَقَلنا الماهية وجب أن نعقل منها أنها لما هي تقتضي ذلك اللازم'.
This is a conditional: if we conceive the essence, we must conceive its demand for the concomitant.
2
Extract the conditional structure
The argument is: If (knowledge of essence), then (knowledge of its necessary concomitant). The justification is that the relation is immediate (without a middle term).
Al-Razi calls this 'بغير وسط' (without a middle).
3
Analyze the objection's logic
The objector says: If knowledge of essence gives knowledge of the proximate concomitant, and that proximate concomitant is a cause of a further concomitant, then we must know all remote concomitants - an impossibility. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The objection targets the chain of entailment: essence → proximate → remote → ...
4
Examine al-Razi's response
Al-Razi replies that knowledge of the cause does not give knowledge of the effect unless we also conceive the effect. Causality is relational ('أمر إضافي'), so both relata must be present.
He uses the example of 'العلة والمعلول' (cause and effect).
5
Evaluate the debate
The objection shows the initial claim is too strong; al-Razi's response effectively restricts the entailment to proximate concomitants conditional on the conception of the effect. Ibn Taymiyya likely presents this to show inconsistency in al-Razi's overall position.
This is a critical refinement: al-Razi's own answer undermines the absolute necessity he initially claimed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان للرازي على هذا التفريق وبيان تناقضه من ثلاثة عشر طريقاً

فالرازي لما ادَّعى ما ادعاه لزمه تناقض زائد على تناقض المنطقيين .

قال الرازي : والبرهان على أن ما يكون لزومه بغير وسط كان بيناً هو أن الماهية لما هي مقتضية لذلك اللازم. فإذا عَقَلنا الماهية وجب أن نعقل منها أنها لما هي تقتضي ذلك اللازم وإلا لما كنا عقلنا الماهية لما هي. وإذا عقلنا منها أنها مقتضية للازم الفلاني وجب أن نعقل اللازم الفلاني، لأن العلم بإضافة أمر إلى أمر يتضمن العلم بكل المُضَافَينِ .

قال : فهذا ما قيل في هذا الباب، وفيه بحث لا بد من ذكره. فإن لقائل أن يقول : إن ما العلم بالماهية لا يقتضي العلم بلازمها يحصل لزومه من العلم بالماهية العلم بلازمها القريب، وذلك القريب علة قريبة للازم الثاني. فحينئذ يجب أن يعلم اللازم الثاني، وأن يعلم من ذلك الثاني اللازم الثالث. فكان يجب أن يكون العلم بالماهية مقتضياً للعلم بجميع لوازمها القريبة والبعيدة. وذلك محال . فثبت أن العلم بالماهية لا يقتضي العلم بلازمها .

قال أيضاً فإن ماهية العلة غير مقولة بالقياس إلى المعلول وعلته، والعلة مقولة بالقياس إلى المعلول. فإذن ماهية العلم مغايرة لعلتها. فإذن لا يلزم من العلم بماهية العلة العلم بدليل المعلول.

ثم قال : لكنا نقول في جواب هذا الشك : العلم بماهية العلة لا يقتضي العلم بالمعلول إلا بشرط آخر، وهو حصول تصور المعلول. فإن العلية أمر إضافي لا يحصل بمجرد ذات العلة، فلا يكون العلم بذات العلة كافياً في العلم بالعلية، لكن ذات العلة وذات [٤٠١] المعلول هما لذاتيهما يقتضيان الوصف الإضافي، وهو العلية والمعلولية لا جرم عند حصول تصورهما يجب حصول التصديق بانتساب أحدهما إلى الآخر. وهذا هو المعنى بقولنا : اللازم الذي يكون بغير وسط يكون بين الثبوت . قال : وإذا ثبت أن تصور العلة إنما يوجب التصديق بثبوت المعلول القريب له عند تصور المعلول ، ثم من الجائز أن لا يحصل تصور المعلول عند تصور العلة، لا جرم لا يلزم من العلم بحقيقة العلة العلم بثبوت كل اللوازم القريبة والبعيدة .

قال : وأما الشك الثاني، فهو أيضاً خارج، لأنا لا نسلّم أن العلية مغايرة لحقيقة ذات العلة.
Proof by al-Razi for this distinction and an explanation of his contradiction from thirteen ways. So al-Razi, when he claimed what he claimed, an additional contradiction beyond the logicians' contradiction became necessary for him. Al-Razi said: The proof that that whose concomitance is without a middle term is evident is that the essence, insofar as it is, necessitates that concomitant. So if we conceive the essence, we must conceive from it that it, insofar as it is, necessitates that concomitant; otherwise we would not have conceived the essence as it is. And if we conceive from it that it necessitates a certain concomitant, we must conceive that certain concomitant, because knowledge of the relation of one thing to another entails knowledge of both relata. He said: This is what has been said in this topic, and there is a discussion that must be mentioned. For someone may say: Knowledge of the essence does not necessitate knowledge of its concomitant; rather, what is obtained from knowledge of the essence is knowledge of its proximate concomitant, and that proximate concomitant is a proximate cause of the second concomitant. Then it would be necessary to know the second concomitant, and from that second one the third concomitant. So knowledge of the essence would necessitate knowledge of all its proximate and remote concomitants. And that is impossible. Therefore it is established that knowledge of the essence does not necessitate knowledge of its concomitant. He also said: Indeed the essence of the cause is not said in relation to the effect and its cause, while the cause is said in relation to the effect. Therefore the essence of knowledge is different from its cause. So knowledge of the essence of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the proof of the effect. Then he said: But we say in answer to this doubt: Knowledge of the essence of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the effect except with another condition, which is obtaining a conception of the effect. For causality is a relational matter that is not obtained merely by the essence of the cause, so knowledge of the essence of the cause is not sufficient for knowledge of causality. But the essence of the cause and the essence of the effect, in their essences, necessitate the relational attribute, which is causality and effectivity; thus when their conceptions are obtained, the affirmation of the relation of one to the other must be obtained. This is the meaning of our saying: The concomitant that is without a middle term is evident in its being established. He said: And when it is established that the conception of the cause only necessitates the affirmation of the existence of its proximate effect when the conception of the effect is present, then it is possible that the conception of the effect is not obtained when the conception of the cause is present; therefore knowledge of the reality of the cause does not necessitate knowledge of the existence of all proximate and remote concomitants. He said: As for the second doubt, it is also invalid, for we do not concede that causality is different from the reality of the essence of the cause.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's attempted proof that knowledge of an essence necessitates knowledge of its necessary concomitant, followed by an objection and al-Razi's response. Ibn Taymiyya is presenting this to show a contradiction in al-Razi's position. Al-Razi argues that if we conceive the essence as it is, we must conceive its demand for the concomitant; otherwise we haven't truly conceived the essence. The objection points out that this leads to an infinite regress: if the essence necessitates knowledge of its proximate concomitant, and that concomitant necessitates another, we would have to know all concomitants, which is impossible. Al-Razi responds by distinguishing that knowledge of the cause does not give knowledge of the effect without a prior concept of the effect; causality is relational. The concomitant that is 'without a middle' is only evident when both relata are conceived. This refutes the objection that we must know all concomitants. Ibn Taymiyya likely cites this to show inconsistency in al-Razi's own system.

Key Terms
الماهية
اللازم
العلة
العلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's response to the objection, knowledge of the cause alone gives knowledge of the effect only when:
A) The effect is necessarily conceived
B) The conception of the effect is also obtained
C) The cause is external
D) The middle term is mental

Key Takeaway
Entailment from essence to concomitant is not absolute but conditional on the conception of the relata in a relational framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an argument similar to al-Razi's using an Islamic legal example: 'Knowledge of the 'illa of wine (intoxication) necessarily entails knowledge that it is haram.' Then apply the objection: if that were true, it would also entail knowledge of all other properties derived from intoxication (e.g., its social harms), which is false. Therefore, the initial claim must be qualified.

Seminar: Is the Essence Cause of its Concomitant?
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' claim that essence is cause of its concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error of conflating concomitance with causation and explain Ibn Taymiyya's counterexample method.

Core Idea
Concomitance does not imply causation; many things are concomitant without one causing the other.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks a cornerstone of the logicians' theory of definition and demonstration: the claim that the essence (mahiyya) is the cause (illa) of its necessary concomitant (lazim). In this dense passage, he systematically dismantles this claim using examples from everyday life, theology, and logic. This lesson will walk you through his method of refutation by counterexample and his deeper arguments about the nature of causation.

Guided Path
1
State the logicians' claim
Identify exactly what Ibn Taymiyya is attacking: the claim that the essence, by virtue of itself, is the efficient cause of its necessary concomitant. This is found at the beginning of the passage.
2
Recognize the fallacy of conflating concomitance with causation
Ibn Taymiyya argues that not everything that is concomitant is caused by its bearer. He gives several examples: fatherhood-sonship, rationality-risibility, etc. These are pairs that always occur together but neither causes the other.
His point is that logical concomitance is not the same as efficient causation.
3
Apply the counterexample method
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya uses diverse examples: from human relationships, attributes, stellar phenomena, bodily humors, and divine attributes. The strategy is to show that if the logicians' universal claim were true, all these pairs would have to be causal, but they are not.
4
Analyze the proof-conclusion relationship
He extends the argument to the domain of proof: a proof (dalil) necessitates its conclusion (madlul), but the proof is not necessarily the cause of the conclusion. Indeed, the conclusion may be more fundamental.
This undermines the logicians' model of demonstration.
5
Summarize the three additional refutations
Ibn Taymiyya anticipates a counter-argument and responds in three ways: (1) the logicians did not really mean necessary attributes; (2) even if they did, the universe is a sign of God but not the cause of His existence; (3) no described entity is an efficient cause of its attributes; it is a locus (mahall).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : فهذا الكلام الذي زعموه أنه برهان قرروا به الأصول الكلية: «الحد» و «البرهان». وفيه من الفساد والخطأ والتناقض ما يطول وصفه، لكن ننبه على بعضه. وذلك من طرق: الطريق الأول: بطلان كون الماهية علة في حصول لازمها. إن قوله: «الماهية لما هي هي مقتضية لذلك اللازم» إن عني به أن الماهية الملزومة هي العلة في حصول لازمها في نفس الأمر، كما يقتضيه كلامه، فهذا من أبطل الباطل. فليس كل ما كان لازماً لغيره يكون ذلك الغير هو العلة المقتضية لوجوده في نفس الأمر. فإن العلة نفسها لازمة لمعلولها المعين، لا يوجد المعلول المعين إلا بتلك العلة. وإن قدر وجود ما هو من جنسه بغير تلك العلة فليس هو ذلك المعين. والعلة لا تكون معلولة لمعلولها، وهي لازمة له. وكذلك أحد المعلولين لا يكون علة للمعلول الآخر كالأبوة والبنوة هما متلازمان وليس وجود أحدهما علة للآخر، بل كلاهما معلول علة أخرى. وكذلك جميع المتلازمات كالناطقية والضاحكية للإنسان متلازمان وليس أحدهما علة للآخر. وكذلك الحس والحركة الإرادية متلازمان وليس أحدهما علة الآخر. ونظير هذا كثير في الحسيات والعقليات والشرعيات، وكل شيء. فإن نجوم الثريا متلازمة ما دام الفلك موجوداً على هذه الصفة وليس بعضها علة لبعض. وكذلك الأخلاط الأربعة في جسد ابن آدم متلازمة وليس بعضها علة لبعض. وصفات الرب تعالى، كعلمه وقدرته، متلازمة وليس إحدى الصفتين علة للأخرى. والأدلة الدالة على الصانع جل جلاله، وهي جميع مخلوقاته ملزومة لوجوده، وليس فيها ما يجوز أن يقال إنه علة له. وبالجملة، فكل دليل فهو مستلزم لمدلوله والمدلول لازم للدليل، وهذا متفق بين العقلاء. ومتفق بين العقلاء على أن الدليل لا يختص بأن يكون معلولاً لمدلوله، بل الدليل أعم. وكل من هذين أمر بين مع اتفاق العقلاء عليه. فكيف يجوز أن يقال: إن كل ما لزم غيره فإن الملزوم هو العلة المقتضية للازم وكل مدلول فهو لازم لدليله مع انتفاء هذا الاقتضاء في أكثر الأدلة؟

فإن قيل: نحن لا نعني باللازم هنا إلا الصفات اللازمة لموصوف، وتلك الماهية الموصوفة مقتضية لذاتها لتلك الصفات. قيل: هذا خطأ من وجوه: أحدها: إنهم لم يعنوا ذلك، كما حكينا كلامهم. بل جعلوا القضايا التي ليست أولية هي ما يفتقر إلى هذا الوسط الذي جعلوه هنا معلولاً للماهية، كما قد حكينا كلامهم. وإن الأوليات حمل محمولها على موضوعها في الوجودين ـ الذهني والخارجي - حملاً أولياً بلا وسط. فجعلوا الوسط الذهني هو الخارجي، والخارجي هو الذي ذكروه هنا وجعلوه معلول الماهية. وأيضاً فإنهم بهذا فرقوا بين اللازم البين واللازم غير البين. الوجه الثاني: أن يقال: هب أنهم أرادوا الصفات اللازمة للماهية، فكل دليل فهو ملزوم لمدلوله. فكونه مستلزماً لمدلوله صفة له لازمة له، وهي على قولهم إما أن تكون بوسط وإما أن تكون بغير وسط. فآيات الله المخلوقة وهي العالم جميعه، مستلزمة لوجود الرب تعالى ووجوده لازم لها، وكونها ملزومة لذلك صفة لها لازمة، لا يتصور قط أن يزول عنها هذا اللازم فإنها مفتقرة بالذات إلى الخالق وهذا الفقر لازم لها لا يفارقها ألبتة. وحدوثها دليل على الباري، وليست هي التي اقتضت وجوده. وكذلك المعلول تقوم به صفات لازمة له وهو وصفاته تستلزم العلة. والمعلول وصفاته من العلة، ليس هو علة لصفاته. الوجه الثالث: أن يقال: لا نسلم أن في الموصوفات ما هو علة فاعلة لصفاته أصلاً، بل هو محل لصفاته القائمة به. والموصوف إن كان هو رب العالمين فلا فاعل لصفاته، كما لا فاعل لذاته. وإن كان مخلوقاً فالفاعل لذاته هو الفاعل لصفاته. أما كون الموصوف علة لصفاته فلا.
I say: This speech which they claim is a demonstration by which they established the universal principles: 'definition' and 'demonstration'. In it there is of corruption, error, and contradiction that would take long to describe, but we shall alert to some of it. And that is through ways: The first way: the invalidity of the essence being a cause in the occurrence of its concomitant. His statement: 'The essence, inasmuch as it is what it is, necessitates that concomitant' – if he means by it that the essence which is the bearer of the concomitant is the cause in the occurrence of its concomitant in reality, as his speech requires, then this is among the most futile of falsehoods. For not everything that is a concomitant of another is such that the other is the cause that necessitates its existence in reality. For the cause itself is a concomitant of its specific effect; the specific effect does not exist except with that cause. If one imagines the existence of something of its type without that cause, it is not that specific thing. And the cause is not an effect of its effect, while it is a concomitant of it. Likewise, one of two effects is not a cause of the other effect – like fatherhood and sonship, they are concomitant, and the existence of neither is a cause of the other, but both are effects of another cause. And similarly all concomitants, like rationality and risibility in a human, they are concomitant and neither is a cause of the other. Likewise, sensation and voluntary movement are concomitant and neither is a cause of the other. And many similar examples exist in sensory, intellectual, and legal matters, and everything. For the stars of the Pleiades are concomitant as long as the celestial sphere exists in this state, and some of them are not a cause of others. Similarly the four humors in the human body are concomitant and some are not a cause of others. And the attributes of the Lord, Exalted – like His knowledge and power – are concomitant and one attribute is not a cause of the other. And the proofs indicating the Creator, Exalted and Magnified – which are all His creations – are necessary consequences of His existence, and among them none may be said to be a cause of Him. In summary, every proof necessitates its conclusion, and the conclusion is a concomitant of the proof, and this is agreed upon by rational people. And it is agreed among rational people that the proof is not exclusive to being an effect of its conclusion; rather, the proof is more general. And each of these two matters is clear with the agreement of rational people. So how can it be said: 'Everything that is a concomitant of another, the bearer is the cause that necessitates the concomitant, and every conclusion is a concomitant of its proof while this necessitation is absent in most proofs?'

If it is said: 'We do not mean by 'concomitant' here except the attributes necessary to a described thing, and that described essence necessitates those attributes by itself.' It is said: This is erroneous in several respects: One of them: They did not mean that, as we have narrated their speech. Rather, they made the propositions that are not axiomatic dependent on this middle term which they have made here an effect of the essence, as we have narrated their speech. And the axiomatic propositions have their predicates predicated of their subjects in both existences – mental and external – with a primary predication without a middle term. So they made the mental middle term the external one, and the external is the one they mentioned here and made it an effect of the essence. Also, by this they distinguished between the obvious concomitant and the non-obvious concomitant. The second aspect: It may be said: Suppose they intended the attributes necessary to the essence, then every proof is a necessary condition for its conclusion. So its being a necessary condition for its conclusion is an attribute necessary to it, which – according to them – is either with a middle term or without a middle term. For the signs of God, the created ones, which are the entire universe, necessitate the existence of the Lord Exalted, and His existence is a concomitant of them, and their being necessary for that is a necessary attribute for them, which can never be imagined to cease from them, for they are inherently in need of the Creator, and this need is a concomitant of them never leaving them at all. Their temporality is a proof of the Creator, and they are not what necessitated His existence. Likewise, the effect has necessary attributes, and it and its attributes necessitate the cause. The effect and its attributes are from the cause, not that it is a cause of its attributes. The third aspect: It may be said: We do not grant that among the described things there is anything that is an efficient cause of its attributes at all; rather, it is a locus for its attributes subsisting in it. The described thing if it is the Lord of the Worlds, then there is no agent of His attributes, just as there is no agent of His essence. And if it is a created thing, then the agent of its essence is the agent of its attributes. As for the described thing being a cause of its attributes, no.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that the essence (mahiyya) of a thing is the cause of its necessary concomitant (lazim). Ibn Taymiyya opens by stating that the logicians' supposed 'demonstration' for the principles of definition and demonstration is full of corruption, error, and contradiction. He will alert to some of it.

He begins with the first way: the invalidity of the notion that the essence is the cause of its concomitant. The logicians had argued that the essence, by virtue of what it is, necessitates its necessary concomitant. Ibn Taymiyya interprets this as meaning that the essence which bears the concomitant is the cause in reality of that concomitant's occurrence. He declares this to be utterly false. He explains: not every concomitant relationship implies that the one thing is the cause of the other. Indeed, the cause itself is concomitant to its specific effect – without the cause the specific effect does not exist. But if something of the same kind occurs without that cause, it is not that specific effect. Moreover, the cause is not an effect of its effect, yet it is concomitant to it.

He then provides numerous examples to demonstrate that concomitance does not entail causation:
- Fatherhood and sonship are concomitant, but neither is the cause of the other; both are effects of a third cause (the causal process of generation).
- Rationality and risibility in humans are concomitant but not causally related.
- Sensation and voluntary movement are concomitant but not causally related.
- The stars of the Pleiades are concomitant as long as the celestial sphere exists, but none is the cause of the others.
- The four humors in the human body are concomitant without one being the cause of another.
- The divine attributes (knowledge and power) are concomitant without one being the cause of the other.
- The proofs of the Creator – all created things – are necessary consequences of His existence, but they are in no way causes of Him.

He summarizes: every proof necessitates its conclusion, and the conclusion is concomitant to the proof. Rational people agree that the proof is not restricted to being an effect of the conclusion; rather, the proof is more general. So how can one claim that every concomitant implies that the bearer is the cause and every conclusion is a concomitant of its proof, while this necessitation is absent in most proofs?

Next, he anticipates a counter-argument: the logicians might say they only mean the necessary attributes of a described entity, and the essence necessitates those attributes by itself. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this in three ways:

First, he argues that the logicians did not mean this, as their own statements show. They set up a middle term (the external existent) which they claimed is an effect of the essence. They also distinguished between the obvious (bayyin) and non-obvious (ghayr bayyin) concomitant using this middle term. Thus, their actual position is not about necessary attributes but about a specific logical structure.

Second, even if we grant that they mean necessary attributes of the essence, consider the signs of God (the universe). They necessitate God's existence, and God's existence is concomitant to them. Their being necessary for God's existence is an attribute necessary to them – it never leaves them, as they are inherently dependent on the Creator. Their temporality proves the Creator, but they do not cause His existence. Similarly, an effect has necessary attributes, and it and those attributes necessitate the cause; but the effect is not the cause of its attributes.

Third, he denies that any described entity is an efficient cause of its attributes. Rather, the entity is a locus (mahall) for the attributes that subsist in it. If the entity is the Lord of the Worlds, He has no agent for His attributes, just as He has no agent for His essence. If it is a created thing, then the agent of its essence is also the agent of its attributes. Therefore, the described entity is not a cause of its attributes.

Thus, Ibn Taymiyya systematically demolishes the logicians' claim, showing that concomitant relationships are far more diverse than their causal model allows.

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya): The essence or quiddity of a thing; what it is in itself. In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' view that the essence is the cause of its necessary concomitants. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
اللازم (al-lāzim): The necessary concomitant or necessary attribute that follows from a subject. Ibn Taymiyya argues that concomitance does not imply causation. [Note: In later logic, al-lāzim is often used in the context of definition (hadd) to denote essential attributes; here Ibn Taymiyya challenges the causal interpretation.]
الملزوم (al-malzūm): The subject or bearer that has a concomitant. Ibn Taymiyya shows that the bearer is not necessarily the cause of the concomitant. [Note: No significant shift; paired with lāzim.]
العلة (al-‘illa): The cause. Here, Ibn Taymiyya denies that the essence (mahiyya) is a cause (especially efficient cause) of its necessary attributes. He restricts causality to agent-causation. [Note: In Islamic philosophy, ‘illa can refer to various types of causes; Ibn Taymiyya's critique focuses on the efficient cause.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

First Objection: Logicians Misidentified the Middle Term
First objection against the logicians' causal claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's first objection against the logicians' claim that the external middle term is an effect of the essence, and explain why their distinction between evident and non-evident necessary concomitants fails.

Core Idea
Necessary concomitance does not imply efficient causation; the logicians confused logical implication with causal production.

In this part of his critique, Ibn Taymiyya targets the logicians' claim that the external middle term is an effect (ma'lul) of the essence. He begins by noting that the relationship between evidence (dalil) and its implication (madlul) is more general than causation. He then addresses an imagined counterargument from the logicians who restrict 'necessary concomitant' to essential attributes. Ibn Taymiyya's first response challenges whether the logicians actually meant this, and points out that their own framework uses the middle term as an effect of the essence, which leads to a flawed distinction between evident and non-evident necessary concomitants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' original claim
Find the claim that the external middle term is an effect of the essence. Ibn Taymiyya states this at the beginning: 'جعلهم الوسط الخارجي معلول الماهية لمدلوله'.
The external middle term is the real-world counterpart of the syllogistic middle term.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's initial objection
He argues that the relationship between evidence and implication is more general than causation. 'بل الدليل أعم' – the evidence is more comprehensive. Many necessary concomitants are not caused by their grounds.
3
Examine the logicians' defense
They claim they only mean the attributes necessary (lazima) for a described thing, and the essence necessitates those attributes. Ibn Taymiyya quotes this: 'نحن لا نعني بـ ((اللازم هنا إلا الصفات اللازمة لموصوف، وتلك الماهية الموصوفة مقتضية لذاتها لتلك الصفات'.
4
Analyze the first objection (الوجه الأول)
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians did not actually mean that; they treated the middle term as an effect of the essence in their own writings. Moreover, they distinguished between evident (bayyin) and non-evident (ghayr bayyin) necessary concomitants using this causal framework, which is inconsistent.
The distinction relies on the middle term being an effect, which is what he is rejecting.
5
Evaluate the outcome
The logicians' defense is invalid because it presupposes the very claim they need to prove: that necessary concomitance is causal. Their distinction between evident and non-evident concomitants is therefore built on a false premise.

Source Text

المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : جعلهم الوسط الخارجي معلول الماهية لمدلوله، بل الدليل أعم. وكل من هذين أمر بين مع اتفاق العقلاء عليه . فكيف يجوز أن يقال: إن كلَّ ما لزم غيره فإن الملزوم هو العلة المقتضية للازم وكل مدلول فهو لازم انتفاء هذا الاقتضاء في أكثر الأدلة؟ لدليله مع أولية هي الخارجي معلول الماهية فإن قيل : نحن لا نعني بـ ((اللازم هنا إلا الصفات اللازمة لموصوف، وتلك الماهية الموصوفة مقتضية لذاتها لتلك الصفات . قيل : هذا خطأ من وجوه : أحدها إنهم لم يعنوا ذلك، كما حكينا كلامهم. بل جعلوا القضايا التي ليست جعلهم الوسط ما يفتقر إلى هذا الوسط الذي جعلوه هنا معلولاً للماهية، كما قد حكينا كلامهم. وإن الأوليات حمل محمولها على موضوعها في الوجودين ـ الذهني والخارجي - حملاً أولياً بلا وسط. فجعلوا الوسط الذهني هو الخارجي، والخارجي هو الذي ذكروه وجعلوه معلول الماهية وأيضاً فإنهم بهذا فرّقوا بين اللازم البين واللازم غير البين . الوجه الثاني أن يقال : هب أنهم أرادوا الصفاتِ اللازمة للماهية، فكل دليل فهو ليس المعلول ملزوم لمدلوله . فكونه مستلزماً لمدلوله صفة له لازمة له، وهي على قولهم إما أن تكون بوسط وإما أن تكون بغير وسط فآيات الله المخلوقة وهي العالم جميعه، مستلزمة لوجود الرب تعالى ووجوده لازم لها، وكونها ملزومة لذلك صفة لها لازمة، لا يتصوّر قط أن يزول عنها هذا اللازم فإنها مفتقرة بالذات إلى الخالق وهذا الفقر لازم لها لا يفارقها ألبتة وحدوثها دليل على الباري، وليست هي التي اقتضت وجوده. وكذلك المعلول تقوم به صفات لازمة له وهو وصفاته تستلزم العلة . والمعلول وصفاته من العلة، ليس هو علة لصفاته. الوجه الثالث أن يقال : لا نسلم أن فى الموصوفات ما هو علة فاعلة لصفته ليس أصلاً، بل هو محل لصفاته القائمة به والموصوف إن كان هو رب العالمين فلا فاعل فاعلاً لصفاته لصفاته، كما لا فاعل لذاته وإن كان مخلوقاً فالفاعل لذاته هو الفاعل لصفاته . أما كون بل هو محل لها ومما يبين بطلانه أن الكلام في صفاته اللازمة، وتلك لا تفارقه، ولا يتصور أن يكون فاعلاً إلا بعد وجودها فيمتنع كونه هو الموجد لها. وأما صفاته المقومة فتلك عندهم هي المقومة لذاته ليست ذاته السابقة لها وهي كالعلة. فجعلوا بعض صفاته اللازمة علة لذاته وهي الصفات المقومة، وبعضها معلولة وهي اللازمة غير المقومة والقسمان خطأ. فليس في صفات الموصوف اللازمة ما هو علة للذات، ولا معلول صفات قائمة بالذات وفاعل الذات فاعلُ صفاتها إن كانت الذات من للذات . بل هي الذوات المفعولة والذي خلق الإنسان خلق الحيوانية والناطقية وخَلَق الضاحكية وغيرها، ليس بعض هذه الصفات سابقاً لذات الإنسان، أو مادة لها، ولا علة لها، ولا بعضها معلول لها. بل هي صفات قائمة بالإنسان لازمة له والذي خلقه خلق هذه الصفات اللازمة له وكذلك سائر المخلوقات .
The Fourth Station - The Ninth Aspect: Their making the external middle term an effect of the essence for its implication, while the evidence is more general. Both of these are clear matters upon which all rational people agree. So how can it be said that every necessary concomitant makes the ground the efficient cause necessitating the consequent, and every implied term is a necessary concomitant, negating this necessity in most evidences? For its evidence with primacy is the external middle term is the effect of the essence. Then if it is said: We mean here by 'necessary concomitant' only the attributes necessary for a described thing, and that described essence is by its essence necessitating those attributes. It is said: This is erroneous in several respects: First, they did not mean that, as we narrated their words. Rather, they made the propositions that are not... they made the middle term that needs this middle term which they made here an effect of the essence, as we have narrated their words. And the primary propositions carry their predicates on their subjects in both existences – mental and external – as a primary carrying without a middle term. So they made the mental middle term the external, and the external is the one they mentioned here and made it an effect of the essence. Also, by this they distinguished between the evident necessary concomitant and the non-evident necessary concomitant. The second aspect: It is said: Suppose they meant the attributes necessary for the essence, then every evidence is not the effect necessitating its implication. So its being necessitating of its implication is an attribute necessary for it, and according to them it is either through a middle term or without a middle term. The signs of Allah – the created universe as a whole – necessitate the existence of the Lord Almighty, and His existence is necessary for them. Their being necessitating of that is an attribute necessary for them, which cannot possibly disappear from them, for they are essentially dependent on the Creator, and this dependency is a necessary concomitant never leaving them. Their temporality is a proof for the Creator, and they are not that which necessitated His existence. Likewise, the effect has necessary attributes inherent in it, and it and its attributes necessitate the cause. The effect and its attributes are from the cause; it is not a cause for its attributes. The third aspect: It is said: We do not concede that among described things there is anything that is an efficient cause for its attribute at all; rather, it is a locus for its attributes subsisting in it. If the described is the Lord of the worlds, then there is no efficient cause for His attributes, as there is no efficient cause for His essence. If it is created, then the efficient cause of its essence is the efficient cause of its attributes. As for it being an efficient cause for its attributes, that is absolutely false. What clarifies its falsehood is that the discussion concerns its necessary attributes, which do not separate from it, and it cannot be imagined to be an efficient cause except after their existence, so it is impossible that it is the one that brought them into existence. As for its constitutive attributes, according to them they are constitutive of its essence; its essence is not prior to them, and they are like the cause. So they made some of its necessary attributes a cause for its essence – these are the constitutive attributes – and some of its attributes effects – these are the necessary non-constitutive attributes. Both categories are erroneous. Therefore, among the necessary attributes of a described thing, there is nothing that is a cause for the essence, nor an effect of attributes subsisting in the essence. The efficient cause of the essence is the efficient cause of its attributes, if the essence is among the effects. Rather, they are the effected essences. The one who created man created animality and rationality and created laughter and others; not some of these attributes are prior to man's essence, nor a matter for it, nor a cause for it, nor are some of them effects for it. Rather, they are attributes subsisting in man, necessary for him, and the one who created him created these necessary attributes for him, and likewise for all other created beings.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that the external middle term in a syllogism is an effect of the essence. He begins by stating the ninth aspect of his critique: the logicians make the external middle term (the real-world correlate of the middle term) an effect of the essence (the nature or quiddity). He points out that the relationship between evidence (daliil) and its implication (madlool) is more general than causation. If every necessary concomitant were caused by its ground, then most evidence would be false, since many implications are not caused by their grounds. He then presents an objection from the logicians: they claim they only mean the essential attributes of a thing, which are necessitated by the essence itself. Ibn Taymiyya responds with three points. First, the logicians did not mean that; they actually treated the middle term as an effect of the essence in their own works, and they distinguished between evident and non-evident necessary concomitants using this framework. Second, even if they meant essential attributes, the relationship of implication is still not causal. For example, the created universe (the signs of Allah) necessitates the existence of the Creator, but the universe does not cause the Creator's existence. The universe's dependency is an inseparable attribute, but it does not make the universe a cause of God. Likewise, an effect has necessary attributes that imply its cause, but the effect is not the cause of those attributes. Third, no described thing is an efficient cause of its own attributes. If the described is God, He has no cause for His attributes. If it is created, the cause of its essence is also the cause of its attributes. It cannot cause its attributes because the attributes are inseparable and cannot be produced after the essence. The logicians' division of attributes into constitutive (which they treat as cause of the essence) and non-constitutive (which are effects) is false. In reality, for created beings like humans, all attributes – animality, rationality, laughing – are created by the same creator who creates the essence; none of them is prior or causal. Thus, the logicians' causal model is rejected.

Key Terms
الوسط الخارجي
المعلول
اللازم
المقتضي
