Second Objection: Implication Without Causation
Second objection: implication does not equal causation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's second objection—that even if the logicians meant essential attributes, the relationship of implication is not causal—using his example of the universe and the Creator.

Core Idea
The universe necessitates the Creator's existence but does not cause it; necessary concomitance is not efficient causation.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique by granting the logicians' assumption that they mean essential attributes. He then argues that even on this interpretation, the claim fails: the fact that a thing implies something does not mean it causes that thing. He provides a powerful example: the created universe (the signs of Allah) necessitates the existence of the Creator, but the universe is not the cause of God's existence. This example clarifies the distinction between logical implication and causal production.

Guided Path
1
Grant the logicians' assumption
Ibn Taymiyya says: 'هب أنهم أرادوا الصفاتِ اللازمة للماهية' – suppose they meant the necessary attributes of the essence. Accept this for the sake of argument.
2
State the consequence
If they meant that, then 'فكل دليل فهو ليس المعلول ملزوم لمدلوله' – every evidence is not an effect necessitating its implication. The implication is a necessary concomitant of the evidence, but not caused by it.
The evidence has a property (being a concomitant) that is necessary, but this does not make it a cause.
3
Analyze the example of the universe
Ibn Taymiyya gives the example: آيات الله المخلوقة (the created signs of Allah, i.e., the universe) are necessary for the existence of the Lord, and His existence is necessary for them. But the universe does not cause God's existence; rather, God causes the universe.
The universe's dependency (fear) is a necessary attribute of it, but the universe is not a cause.
4
Generalize to effects and causes
An effect (ma'lul) has necessary attributes that imply its cause, but the effect is not the cause of those attributes. The cause produces the effect and its attributes.
5
Conclude the second objection
Thus, even if we accept the logicians' narrower definition, the relationship remains one of implication, not efficient causation. The universe's temporality is a proof for the Creator, but it does not necessitate the Creator's existence in a causal sense.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this to show that the logicians' causal model is not needed for valid inference.

المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : جعلهم الوسط الخارجي معلول الماهية لمدلوله، بل الدليل أعم. وكل من هذين أمر بين مع اتفاق العقلاء عليه . فكيف يجوز أن يقال: إن كلَّ ما لزم غيره فإن الملزوم هو العلة المقتضية للازم وكل مدلول فهو لازم انتفاء هذا الاقتضاء في أكثر الأدلة؟ لدليله مع أولية هي الخارجي معلول الماهية فإن قيل : نحن لا نعني بـ ((اللازم هنا إلا الصفات اللازمة لموصوف، وتلك الماهية الموصوفة مقتضية لذاتها لتلك الصفات . قيل : هذا خطأ من وجوه : أحدها إنهم لم يعنوا ذلك، كما حكينا كلامهم. بل جعلوا القضايا التي ليست جعلهم الوسط ما يفتقر إلى هذا الوسط الذي جعلوه هنا معلولاً للماهية، كما قد حكينا كلامهم. وإن الأوليات حمل محمولها على موضوعها في الوجودين ـ الذهني والخارجي - حملاً أولياً بلا وسط. فجعلوا الوسط الذهني هو الخارجي، والخارجي هو الذي ذكروه وجعلوه معلول الماهية وأيضاً فإنهم بهذا فرّقوا بين اللازم البين واللازم غير البين . الوجه الثاني أن يقال : هب أنهم أرادوا الصفاتِ اللازمة للماهية، فكل دليل فهو ليس المعلول ملزوم لمدلوله . فكونه مستلزماً لمدلوله صفة له لازمة له، وهي على قولهم إما أن تكون بوسط وإما أن تكون بغير وسط فآيات الله المخلوقة وهي العالم جميعه، مستلزمة لوجود الرب تعالى ووجوده لازم لها، وكونها ملزومة لذلك صفة لها لازمة، لا يتصوّر قط أن يزول عنها هذا اللازم فإنها مفتقرة بالذات إلى الخالق وهذا الفقر لازم لها لا يفارقها ألبتة وحدوثها دليل على الباري، وليست هي التي اقتضت وجوده. وكذلك المعلول تقوم به صفات لازمة له وهو وصفاته تستلزم العلة . والمعلول وصفاته من العلة، ليس هو علة لصفاته. الوجه الثالث أن يقال : لا نسلم أن فى الموصوفات ما هو علة فاعلة لصفته ليس أصلاً، بل هو محل لصفاته القائمة به والموصوف إن كان هو رب العالمين فلا فاعل فاعلاً لصفاته لصفاته، كما لا فاعل لذاته وإن كان مخلوقاً فالفاعل لذاته هو الفاعل لصفاته . أما كون بل هو محل لها ومما يبين بطلانه أن الكلام في صفاته اللازمة، وتلك لا تفارقه، ولا يتصور أن يكون فاعلاً إلا بعد وجودها فيمتنع كونه هو الموجد لها. وأما صفاته المقومة فتلك عندهم هي المقومة لذاته ليست ذاته السابقة لها وهي كالعلة. فجعلوا بعض صفاته اللازمة علة لذاته وهي الصفات المقومة، وبعضها معلولة وهي اللازمة غير المقومة والقسمان خطأ. فليس في صفات الموصوف اللازمة ما هو علة للذات، ولا معلول صفات قائمة بالذات وفاعل الذات فاعلُ صفاتها إن كانت الذات من للذات . بل هي الذوات المفعولة والذي خلق الإنسان خلق الحيوانية والناطقية وخَلَق الضاحكية وغيرها، ليس بعض هذه الصفات سابقاً لذات الإنسان، أو مادة لها، ولا علة لها، ولا بعضها معلول لها. بل هي صفات قائمة بالإنسان لازمة له والذي خلقه خلق هذه الصفات اللازمة له وكذلك سائر المخلوقات .
The Fourth Station - The Ninth Aspect: Their making the external middle term an effect of the essence for its implication, while the evidence is more general. Both of these are clear matters upon which all rational people agree. So how can it be said that every necessary concomitant makes the ground the efficient cause necessitating the consequent, and every implied term is a necessary concomitant, negating this necessity in most evidences? For its evidence with primacy is the external middle term is the effect of the essence. Then if it is said: We mean here by 'necessary concomitant' only the attributes necessary for a described thing, and that described essence is by its essence necessitating those attributes. It is said: This is erroneous in several respects: First, they did not mean that, as we narrated their words. Rather, they made the propositions that are not... they made the middle term that needs this middle term which they made here an effect of the essence, as we have narrated their words. And the primary propositions carry their predicates on their subjects in both existences – mental and external – as a primary carrying without a middle term. So they made the mental middle term the external, and the external is the one they mentioned here and made it an effect of the essence. Also, by this they distinguished between the evident necessary concomitant and the non-evident necessary concomitant. The second aspect: It is said: Suppose they meant the attributes necessary for the essence, then every evidence is not the effect necessitating its implication. So its being necessitating of its implication is an attribute necessary for it, and according to them it is either through a middle term or without a middle term. The signs of Allah – the created universe as a whole – necessitate the existence of the Lord Almighty, and His existence is necessary for them. Their being necessitating of that is an attribute necessary for them, which cannot possibly disappear from them, for they are essentially dependent on the Creator, and this dependency is a necessary concomitant never leaving them. Their temporality is a proof for the Creator, and they are not that which necessitated His existence. Likewise, the effect has necessary attributes inherent in it, and it and its attributes necessitate the cause. The effect and its attributes are from the cause; it is not a cause for its attributes. The third aspect: It is said: We do not concede that among described things there is anything that is an efficient cause for its attribute at all; rather, it is a locus for its attributes subsisting in it. If the described is the Lord of the worlds, then there is no efficient cause for His attributes, as there is no efficient cause for His essence. If it is created, then the efficient cause of its essence is the efficient cause of its attributes. As for it being an efficient cause for its attributes, that is absolutely false. What clarifies its falsehood is that the discussion concerns its necessary attributes, which do not separate from it, and it cannot be imagined to be an efficient cause except after their existence, so it is impossible that it is the one that brought them into existence. As for its constitutive attributes, according to them they are constitutive of its essence; its essence is not prior to them, and they are like the cause. So they made some of its necessary attributes a cause for its essence – these are the constitutive attributes – and some of its attributes effects – these are the necessary non-constitutive attributes. Both categories are erroneous. Therefore, among the necessary attributes of a described thing, there is nothing that is a cause for the essence, nor an effect of attributes subsisting in the essence. The efficient cause of the essence is the efficient cause of its attributes, if the essence is among the effects. Rather, they are the effected essences. The one who created man created animality and rationality and created laughter and others; not some of these attributes are prior to man's essence, nor a matter for it, nor a cause for it, nor are some of them effects for it. Rather, they are attributes subsisting in man, necessary for him, and the one who created him created these necessary attributes for him, and likewise for all other created beings.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that the external middle term in a syllogism is an effect of the essence. He begins by stating the ninth aspect of his critique: the logicians make the external middle term (the real-world correlate of the middle term) an effect of the essence (the nature or quiddity). He points out that the relationship between evidence (daliil) and its implication (madlool) is more general than causation. If every necessary concomitant were caused by its ground, then most evidence would be false, since many implications are not caused by their grounds. He then presents an objection from the logicians: they claim they only mean the essential attributes of a thing, which are necessitated by the essence itself. Ibn Taymiyya responds with three points. First, the logicians did not mean that; they actually treated the middle term as an effect of the essence in their own works, and they distinguished between evident and non-evident necessary concomitants using this framework. Second, even if they meant essential attributes, the relationship of implication is still not causal. For example, the created universe (the signs of Allah) necessitates the existence of the Creator, but the universe does not cause the Creator's existence. The universe's dependency is an inseparable attribute, but it does not make the universe a cause of God. Likewise, an effect has necessary attributes that imply its cause, but the effect is not the cause of those attributes. Third, no described thing is an efficient cause of its own attributes. If the described is God, He has no cause for His attributes. If it is created, the cause of its essence is also the cause of its attributes. It cannot cause its attributes because the attributes are inseparable and cannot be produced after the essence. The logicians' division of attributes into constitutive (which they treat as cause of the essence) and non-constitutive (which are effects) is false. In reality, for created beings like humans, all attributes – animality, rationality, laughing – are created by the same creator who creates the essence; none of them is prior or causal. Thus, the logicians' causal model is rejected.

Key Terms

المعلول
اللازم
المقتضي

C) The middle term and the minor term
D) Efficient cause and effect

Key Takeaway
The second objection establishes that necessary concomitance is a logical relation, not a causal one, using the clear example of the universe and its Creator.

15 min
Third Objection: Described Things Are Not Efficient Causes of Their Attributes
Third objection: no described thing is efficient cause of its attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's third objection: that no described thing (whether Creator or created) is an efficient cause of its own attributes, and his critique of the logicians' division of attributes into constitutive and non-constitutive.

Core Idea
Attributes do not cause essences, nor are they caused by essences; both essences and attributes are created by Allah alone.

In his third objection, Ibn Taymiyya delivers a decisive blow to the logicians' causal model. He argues that no described thing (موصوف) is an efficient cause of its own attributes. If the described is God, He has no efficient cause for His attributes, just as He has no cause for His essence. If it is created, then the efficient cause of its essence is also the efficient cause of its attributes. The logicians' division of attributes into constitutive (مقومة) and non-constitutive (لازمة غير مقومة) is also false, as it treats some attributes as causes of the essence and others as effects, which is incoherent. Using the example of man, he shows that all attributes—animality, rationality, laughter—are created by the same Creator who created the essence; none is prior or causal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the universal denial
Ibn Taymiyya states: 'لا نسلم أن فى الموصوفات ما هو علة فاعلة لصفته' – we do not concede that any described thing is an efficient cause of its attribute. This is the core claim.
2
Distinguish between God and creation
If the described is God, there is no efficient cause for His attributes, just as there is none for His essence. If it is created, the cause of the essence is also the cause of the attributes. The created thing cannot cause its own attributes because they are inseparable and cannot be produced after the essence.
3
Examine the logicians' division of attributes
The logicians divide attributes into constitutive (مقومة) which they treat as cause of the essence, and non-constitutive necessary attributes (لازمة غير مقومة) which they treat as effects. Ibn Taymiyya rejects both categories: no attribute is a cause of the essence, and no attribute is an effect of the essence.
He says: 'فجعلوا بعض صفاته اللازمة علة لذاته وهي الصفات المقومة، وبعضها معلولة وهي اللازمة غير المقومة والقسمان خطأ'.
4
Analyze the example of man
Ibn Taymiyya gives a concrete example: man (al-insan). The one who created man created animality (al-hayawaniyya), rationality (al-natiqiyya), the capacity to laugh (al-dahikiyya), and all other attributes. None of these attributes is prior to man's essence, nor a matter (madda) for it, nor a cause for it, nor an effect of it. They are all created by Allah.
This shows that the logicians' causal framework is incompatible with created beings.
5
Conclude the third objection
Thus, the logicians' claim that the external middle term is an effect of the essence is baseless. The correct view is that necessary attributes are simply attributes of the thing, created by Allah, and they do not stand in causal relations with the essence.

Disambiguating 'Muqtadiya': Implication vs. Causation
Ibn Taymiyya gives a fourth objection and a second argument against the logicians' causal interpretation of necessary concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between logical implication (luzum) and causal efficacy (fa'iliyya) in Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' claim about essence and attributes.

Core Idea
Implying is not causing: Ibn Taymiyya exposes the logicians' conflation of mere necessity with causal production.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya exposes a critical ambiguity in the logicians' claim that a quiddity (mahiyya) is a 'muqtadiya' of its attributes. He challenges them to specify whether they mean mere implication or actual causation. This distinction is foundational for understanding how Islamic scholars evaluate arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where conflating implication with causation can lead to invalid inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find the logician's statement that the essence is muqtadiya of its attributes. This is the claim under attack.
The logicians assert that the mahiyya necessitates its attributes, but they often imply a causal relationship.
2
Unpack the two possible meanings
Ibn Taymiyya gives two interpretations: istilzam (implication) and fa'iliyya (efficient causation). List them clearly.
The Arabic term muqtadiya can carry both senses, leading to equivocation.
3
Evaluate the first meaning
If they mean mere implication, then it is trivial: all essences imply their attributes, so no distinction is made. This does not support the logicians' claim.
Ibn Taymiyya says 'all necessitators are like that' – the statement becomes universally true and thus empty.
4
Evaluate the second meaning
If they mean efficient causation, Ibn Taymiyya says it is impossible because no essence performs an act on its own attributes.
Attributes are inseparable from the essence; they cannot be caused by that same essence.
5
Apply the second approach
Even granting the causal interpretation, the only clear meaning is the conditional: whenever the essence exists, the attribute exists. This does not prove causation.
Most necessary concomitants are not effects of their necessitators; only some specific cases (like effect from cause) show causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع أن يقال : ما تعني بقولك وتلك الماهية الموصوفة مقتضية لصفاتها؟ إن عنيتَ أنها مستلزمة لها فحينئذٍ جميع الملزومات كذلك كما تقدم، فبطل كونهم أرادوا بعض الملزومات. وإن عنيت أنها فاعلة للزومها فليس في الماهيات ما يفعل شيئاً من صفاتها . الطريق الثاني أن يقال : لو سلم أنها مقتضية لذلك اللازم بمعنى أنها علة كما ادعاه، أو بمعنى ليست معلولة لملزوماتها أنها ملزومة لذلك اللازم كما هو الحقُّ في نفس الأمر ، فإن كون الملزوم ملزوماً واللازم ثابت لا ريب فيه . وأما كون الملزوم فَعَل اللازم، أو علة للازم، فهذا قد يكون في بعض الملزومات كالمعلول المعيَّن اللازم لعلته وإلا فأكثر اللوازم ليست معلولة لملزوماتها . وسواء قُدّر أنها معلولة أو لم يقدر، فقوله: «الماهية لما مقتضية لذلك اللازم كلام مجمل فإن كون الشيء ملزوماً لغيره هو مقتضية لذلك اللازم كلام فيه أنه متى تحقق الملزوم تحقق اللازم. فقوله: «الماهية لما هي هي مقتضية لذلك اللازم كلام مجمل .
The fourth aspect: It is said: What do you mean by your statement that the described essence is necessitator of its attributes? If you mean it is necessitating them, then all necessitators are like that, as previously, so their intending some necessitators is false. If you mean it is an efficient cause of their necessity, then among essences there is nothing that does anything from its attributes. The second approach: It is said: Even if it is granted that it is a necessitator of that necessary concomitant in the sense that it is a cause as he claimed, or in the sense that it is not an effect of its necessitators that it is necessitator of that necessary concomitant as is the truth in reality, then the fact that the necessitator is a necessitator and the necessary concomitant is established is indubitable. As for the necessitator being the doer of the necessary concomitant, or a cause for the necessary concomitant, this may occur in some necessitators, like the specific effect necessitated by its cause. Otherwise, most necessary concomitants are not effects of their necessitators. Whether it is assumed that they are effects or not, his statement: 'The essence, because it is what it is, is a necessitator of that necessary concomitant' is an ambiguous statement. Indeed, the fact that a thing is a necessitator of another means that whenever the necessitator is realized, the necessary concomitant is realized. So his statement: 'The essence, because it is what it is, is a necessitator of that necessary concomitant' is an ambiguous expression.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage contains Ibn Taymiyya's fourth objection and a second overarching argument against the logicians' causal interpretation of necessary concomitance. In the fourth objection, he challenges the logician's statement that 'the described essence is a necessitator of its attributes'. He distinguishes two possible meanings: (1) that the essence simply necessitates (implies) its attributes, which is trivial and does not distinguish one necessitator from another, so the logician's claim that some things (like the middle term) are effects is false; (2) that the essence is an efficient cause (faa'il) of the necessity of its attributes, which is impossible because no essence performs any act on its own attributes. Then he presents the second approach (al-tariq al-thani): even if we grant the logician's claim that the essence is a necessitator in the sense of being a cause, or in the sense of not being an effect, we must distinguish between the mere relation of implication (luzum) and causal efficacy. The relation of implication is simply that whenever the necessitator exists, the necessary concomitant exists. But whether the necessitator actually causes or produces the necessary concomitant is a separate issue. In some cases – like a specific effect necessitated by its cause – causation holds, but for most implications there is no causal relationship. Therefore, the logician's phrase 'the essence, because it is what it is, is a necessitator of that necessary concomitant' is ambiguous (mujmal) because it conflates mere implication with causation. The only clear meaning is the conditional one: if the essence exists, the concomitant exists. No causal link is established.

Key Terms
مقتضية
فاعلة للزومها
كلام مجمل

D) The concomitant is an effect of the essence.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya forces a precise distinction: the relationship between an essence and its attributes is one of implication (luzum), not causation, unless proven otherwise.

Fifth Refutation: The Regress Argument – Immediate Concomitants Entail Infinite Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya's fifth refutation: regress argument against immediate concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's reductio ad absurdum against the claim that conceiving a quiddity entails knowing its immediate necessary concomitant, and apply this critique to a theological argument.

Core Idea
If knowing one thing required knowing its immediate concomitants, you would have to know everything — an absurdity.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' assumption that when a quiddity is conceived, its immediate necessary concomitants (lazim bi-ghayr wasat) are automatically known. He demonstrates that this leads to an infinite regress and, more absurdly, that knowing any creature would entail knowing God and all His attributes. This is a classic reductio ad absurdum that exposes the logical flaw in the conception theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Logicians claim that conceiving a quiddity entails knowing its immediate necessary concomitants without need for proof.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's conditional
If that claim were true, then each concomitant would itself have an immediate concomitant, leading to an infinite chain.
3
Derive the theological absurdity
Since creatures entail the Creator, knowing any creature would force knowledge of God and all His works.
4
Recognize the reductio structure
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the consequence is impossible, so the original claim is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن من تصور جميع لوازمه الطريق الخامس قوله يستلزم أن يقال : لو كان ما ذكرته صحيحاً للزم أن من عقل شيئاً عقل لوازم الشيء شيئاً تصور جميعها. فإنه إذا عقل الملزوم وجب أن يعقل لازمه الذي بغير وسط. ثم عقل ذلك [٤١٠] اللازم يوجب عقل الآخر، وهلم جراً. فيلزم أن يكون قد عقل اللوازم جميعها . وهذا كما أنه يبطل حجته فهو يبطل أصل قولهم : إن اللازم بغير وسط لا يفتقر إلى دليل في حق أحد . الله عرف جميع أن فإنه لو كان كذلك لكان من تصور شيئاً تصور جميع لوازمه. ويلزم أن من عَرَف ما يعلمه الله ، فيعلم كلَّ شيء، لأن علمه سبحانه من لوازم ذاته . بل يلزم من علم شيئاً من مخلوقاته علم كل شيء، فإن المخلوق مستلزم للخالق، والخالق مستلزم لعلمه بكل شيء بل وهو مستلزم لمشيئته ومشيئته مستلزمة لجميع ما خلقه، فإنه ما شاء كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن . فلو كان مَنْ علم الملزوم لا بد أن يعلم لازمه لزم أن مَنْ علم شيئاً فقد علم كل شيء وفساد هذا بين، مع 6 أن فساد هذه الدعوى أظهر من أن يحتاج إلى دليل، لكن إذا عُرِف شَنَاعةُ لوازمها كان أدلَّ على قبحها وفسادها. وهذا يتضمن السؤال الذي ذكره وقال: «فيه بحث لا بد من ذكره كما تقدم حكاية لفظه والجواب الذي ذكره عنه باطل، وقد تناقض فيه . وذلك يتبين بذكر الطريق السادس)
That whoever conceives all its necessary concomitants – the fifth method – his statement entails that it be said: If what you mentioned were correct, then it would follow that whoever intellets something intellets its necessary concomitants successively [until] he conceives all of them. For when he intellets the substrate, he must intellect its necessary concomitant without a middle term. Then intelleting that necessary concomitant obliges intelleting the other, and so on. So it would follow that he has intellected all the necessary concomitants. This, just as it invalidates his argument, invalidates the basis of their statement: that the necessary concomitant without a middle term does not require a proof for anyone. For if it were so, then whoever conceives something would conceive all its necessary concomitants. And it would follow that whoever knows what God knows, he knows everything, because His knowledge, exalted, is among the necessary concomitants of His essence. Rather, it would follow that whoever knows something from His creatures knows everything, for the creature entails the Creator, and the Creator entails His knowledge of everything, and indeed He entails His will, and His will entails all that He created, for what He wills is and what He does not will is not. So if whoever knows the substrate must know its necessary concomitant, then it would follow that whoever knows something has known everything. The corruption of this is evident, and indeed the corruption of this claim is clearer than to need proof, but when the heinousness of its implications is known, it is more indicative of its ugliness and corruption. This includes the question he mentioned and said: 'There is a discussion that must be mentioned as previously narrated in his words, and the answer he gave about it is false, and he contradicted himself in it. That will be clarified by mentioning the sixth method.'
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's fifth argument, which is a reductio ad absurdum of the logicians' position. He begins by stating the consequence: if the claim that conceiving a quiddity requires conceiving its immediate necessary concomitant (without a middle term) were true, then it would lead to an infinite regress. For each concomitant, when intellected, would itself require its own immediate concomitant, and so on, so that eventually all necessary concomitants would have to be known. He then points out that this contradicts the logicians' own position that an immediate necessary concomitant does not require a proof (daliil) for anyone. But if the chain holds, then knowing one thing would require knowing an infinite number of things, which is impossible. Ibn Taymiyya then draws a theological implication: it would absurdly imply that knowing any creature entails knowing God and all His attributes and actions, which is clearly false. For example, knowing a human does not automatically give knowledge of the Creator and all His works. He notes that the corruption of this claim is obvious, and he signals that there is a further objection (the question) to which the logician gave a false answer, which will be exposed in the sixth method.

Key Terms
لازم بغير وسط
دور

D) That necessary concomitants require proof.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses a regress argument to show that the logicians' claim about immediate necessary concomitants leads to an absurd requirement of knowing everything.

Refuting the Identity of Mental and External Middle Terms
Refutation of identifying mental and external middle terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against identifying the mental middle term with the external middle term and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The mental middle term needs two concepts for knowledge; external entailment needs only one – they cannot be identical.

This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critical refutation of a key claim in Aristotelian logic: that the middle term used in mental reasoning is exactly the same as a cause in external reality. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the conditions for knowing an entailment in the mind differ fundamentally from the conditions for that entailment to hold externally. Understanding this critique is essential for distinguishing between logical operations in the mind and real-world causal relationships, a distinction with profound implications for Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
State the target claim
Identify the doctrine that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking: the claim that the mental middle term (al-wasat al-dhihni) is identical to the external middle term (al-wasat al-khariji). This is the claim that a mental concept used to connect premises corresponds exactly to a real cause in external reality.
The logicians assert that what mediates in the mind is the same as what mediates in existence.
2
Follow the deconstruction
Ibn Taymiyya points out an inconsistency: The logicians hold that when entailment requires a middle term, knowledge of that entailment requires that middle term. But when they try to know the entailment of the mental middle term, they require conceiving both cause and effect (two conceptions). Meanwhile, the external entailment depends only on the cause (one). This mismatch shows the mental and external middle terms cannot be identical.
The logicians' own reasoning leads to a contradiction: the conditions for knowledge in the mind differ from the conditions for existence in the external world.
3
Identify the alternative implied
Ibn Taymiyya does not explicitly state an alternative, but the critique implies that the mental middle term is a mere conceptual tool, not a real external entity. The mental process involves both cause and effect to know entailment, while external causality involves only the cause.
This distinction preserves the independence of external reality from mental operations.
4
Evaluate the critique's validity
Assess whether Ibn Taymiyya's argument is sound. He uses the logicians' own claims against them, showing an inconsistency. The argument is valid because it correctly identifies a mismatch in the number of relata required for mental knowledge versus external entailment.
The critique is internally consistent and does not rely on external assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : الوسط الذهني هو الخارجي، باطل

إبطال القول بأن الوسط الذهني هو الخارجي، وأنتم جعلتم ما كان يفتقر لزومه إلى وسط افتقر العلم به إلى وسط، وما لزم بلا وسط عُلم بلا وسط . فإذا جعلتم العلم بلزومه موقوفاً على تصور المعلول والعلة والملزوم واللازم جميعاً وقلتم لا يُعلم اللزوم إلا بعد هذين التصورين، واللزوم الخارجي موقوف على العلة وحدها لا على الاثنين بطل قولكم : «إن الوسط الذهني هو الخارجي». فإن هذا يتوقف العلم بلزومه على تصورين ولزومه في الخارج إنما لازم لواحد، لا لاثنين.
The fourth station - the ninth aspect: The mental middle term is the external [middle term] is false. [This is] the refutation of the statement that the mental middle term is the external [middle term]. You made what required a middle term for its entailment require a middle term for knowledge of it, and what entailed without a middle term is known without a middle term. So when you made knowledge of its entailment dependent on conceiving of the effect and the cause and the entailed and the entailer all together, and you said 'the entailment is not known except after these two conceptions', while the external entailment depends on the cause alone, not on the two, then your statement 'the mental middle term is the external [middle term]' is false. For the knowledge of its entailment depends on two conceptions, while its entailment in the external is only an entailer for one, not for two.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that the mental middle term (al-wasat al-dhihni) is identical to the external middle term (al-wasat al-khariji). The author, Ibn Taymiyya, first states the claim and then argues against it. He points out that the logicians' reasoning is inconsistent: they claim that when an entailment requires a middle term, knowledge of that entailment requires that middle term, but when entailment occurs without a middle term, it is known without one. Then, when they make knowledge of entailment dependent on conceiving both the effect and the cause (i.e., two conceptions), yet the external entailment depends only on the cause alone—not on both—their claim that the mental middle term is the external middle term collapses. The key point is that the mental process involves two elements (cause and effect) to know the entailment, whereas in external reality, the entailment is only from the cause to the effect, so the mental middle term cannot be the same as the external one.

الوسط الذهني
الوسط الخارجي
اللزوم

The Eighth Path: Relational Matters – Mental vs. External Existence
Author distinguishes mental from external existence for relational matters, showing one thing can be cause of many.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental and external existence for relational attributes and use this distinction to refute claims that relational matters require two entities externally.

Core Idea
One cause can yield two relational attributes; the mistake is confusing mental with external existence.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes on a common logical principle: that relational matters require two things. He sharpens the analysis by asking a critical question: do you mean in the mind or in reality? This distinction is crucial for avoiding equivocation. By the end, you will see how a single cause – like God's essence – can give rise to multiple relational attributes without needing two separate entities to exist first.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent states that 'relational matters (al-umūr al-iḍāfiyya) are not sufficiently obtained by a single thing; both relata must be obtained.' This is the target of critique.
2
Distinguish the two senses
Ibn Taymiyya asks: do you mean in the mind or in the external world? In the mind, it is true: causality requires conceiving both cause and effect. But in external reality, it is false.
This is the key move – exposing the equivocation.
3
Apply the distinction to the divine example
God's essence alone entails all His attributes, including relational ones like being Creator. No rational person says the created makes the Creator a creator. The relation exists independently of the created's existence.
Even if one holds that creation is identical to the created, the cause-effect priority remains.
4
Consider the father-son example
Fatherhood and sonship are correlatives that are both necessitated by a single cause: procreation. The procreative act produces both relata without requiring the son's existence for the father's causality.
This shows a single cause can produce multiple relational attributes externally.
5
Assess Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
The opponent's argument is valid only in the mental realm. By conflating the two realms, he commits taghlīṭ (deception). Ibn Taymiyya provides three responses: the opponent either meant mental, or meant external but is wrong, or meant something else that does not benefit him.
This dismantles the universal claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثامن

قولك : الأمور الإضافية لا يكفي في حصولها الشيء الواحد) تعني به في الذهن أو في الخارج؟ فإن هذا الموضع فيه التباس. إن عنيت أنها لا تتصور إلا بتصور شيئين، ولا ريب فهذا صحيح، أن العلية لا تتصور إلا بتصور العلة والمعلول، فحصولها في التصور الذهني مشروط بحصول تصور المضافين في الذهن. وإن عنيت أنها لا توجد في الخارج إلا بالشيئين فهذا باطل بالضرورة واتفاق العقلاء. فإن ذات الرب وحدها مستلزمة لكل ما يتصف به من الأمور الثبوتية والإضافية. وهذا متفق عليه بين المسلمين والفلاسفة، وسائر الناس . فإن كونه خالقاً للعالم، ورباً، وفاعلاً ، هو الذي يسمونه «علة» و «مؤثراً». والناس متنازعون في «الخلق» هل هو أمر عدمي، وهو نفس المخلوق كما يقوله أكثر المعتزلة والأشعرية أم «الخلق» زائد على «المخلوق» كما هو قول جماهير المسلمين، وهو قول السلف والأئمة وذكر البخاري أنه قول العلماء مطلقاً لم يذكر فيه نزاعاً. وقد بسط الكلام على هذا في غير هذا الموضع. والمقصود هنا أنه على كل قول فلا يقول عاقل : إن المخلوق أوجب كونه خالقاً، ولا المعلول جعل العلة علة، ولا أنه شرط في ذلك . بل الخالق سبحانه هو وحده خَلَق المخلوقات . وكونه خالقاً — على قول الجمهور — حاصل بقدرته ومشيئته جميعاً عند المسلمين، ليس خاصاً بالمخلوقات. بل خَلْقُ المخلوق متقدم عليه في نفس الأمر. فكيف يكون «الخلق معلولاً لـ «المخلوق؟ وكذلك علية العلة متقدمة على المعلول في نفس الأمر. فكيف يكون المعلولُ جزءً علية العلة؟ فقوله «إن العلية أمر إضافي، والأمور الإضافية لا يكفي في حصولها الشيء الواحد، بل لا بد من حصول كلا المضافين فيه تغليط بسبب الإجمال في قوله، فإن ما ذكره صحيح في الحصول في العلم والتصور. وأما الحصول الخارجي فالشيء الواحد قد يكون علة لأمور كثيرة إضافية والعلة علة المعلول، وعله عليتها، وهما جميعاً حاصلان بالعلة. وكذلك الأبوة والبنوة من المتضائفات وهما لازمان للإيلاد. فَعِلَّة الإيلاد أوجبت هذين المتضائفين .

فإن قيل : أراد أن الأمور الإضافية لا توجد إلا بوجود المضافين وإن كان أحدهما مستقلاً بوجود المضافين قيل عن هذا جوابان: أحدهما: بأنه لم يُرد هذا. ولو أراده لم ينفعه، وكان خطأ. فهذه ثلاثة أجوبة .
The eighth path: Your statement: 'Relational matters are not sufficiently obtained by a single thing'—do you mean in the mind or in the external? For this place has ambiguity. If you mean that they are not conceived except by conceiving two things, then there is no doubt that this is correct: causality is not conceived except by conceiving the cause and the effect. So its occurrence in mental conception is conditioned by the occurrence of the conception of the two relata in the mind. But if you mean that they do not exist in the external except by two things, then this is false by necessity and by the agreement of the rational people. For the Essence of the Lord alone entails all the positive and relational attributes that He is described with. This is agreed upon between Muslims, philosophers, and all people. For His being Creator of the world, Lord, and Agent is what they call 'cause' and 'efficient'. People disagree about 'creation': is it a non-existential matter, namely the created itself, as most Mu'tazila and Ash'aris say, or is 'creation' additional to 'the created' as the majority of Muslims say, and it is the saying of the Salaf and Imams, and al-Bukhari mentioned that it is the saying of the scholars absolutely without mentioning dispute. The discussion on this has been expanded elsewhere. The purpose here is that on any saying, no rational person says that the created necessitates its being Creator, nor that the effect makes the cause a cause, nor that it is a condition for that. Rather, the Creator, glorified, alone created the creatures. And His being Creator—according to the majority—occurs by His power and will together according to Muslims, not specific to creatures. Rather, the creation of the created is prior to it in reality. So how can 'creation' be an effect of 'the created'? Likewise, the causality of the cause is prior to the effect in reality. So how can the effect be part of the causality of the cause? His statement 'causality is a relational matter, and relational matters are not sufficiently obtained by a single thing, but both relata must be obtained' contains deception due to equivocation in his statement: what he mentioned is correct regarding occurrence in knowledge and conception. But as for external occurrence, a single thing may be a cause for many relational matters: the cause is the cause of the effect and the cause of its own causality, and both are obtained by the cause. Likewise, fatherhood and sonship are correlatives (mutadhā'ifāt) and both are necessary consequences of procreation. So the cause of procreation necessitated these two correlatives.

If it is said: 'He meant that relational matters only exist with the existence of the two relata, even if one of them is independent in the existence of the two relata,' it is said about this two answers: One: that he did not mean this. 'And if he meant it, it would not benefit him, and would be an error.' So these are three answers.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that relational matters (umūr iḍāfiyya) require two things to exist. Ibn Taymiyya begins by asking whether the opponent means in the mind or in the external world. If in the mind, it is correct that relational matters like causality require conceiving both cause and effect. If in external existence, it is false and contradicted by necessity and universal agreement. He provides the example of God: His Essence alone entails all His attributes, including relational ones like being Creator. Even among the disagreement about whether creation is identical to the created or additional, no rational person claims that the created makes the Creator a creator. In reality, creation precedes the created, so the created cannot be a condition for creation. Similarly, causality precedes the effect. The opponent's statement is deceptive because it equivocates between mental and external existence. Ibn Taymiyya then gives the example of fatherhood and sonship: these are correlatives that come from the single cause of procreation. The cause of procreation produces both simultaneously, without requiring the existence of the son for the causality. He then addresses a possible rejoinder: if the opponent meant that relational matters require both relata in existence, even if one is independent, Ibn Taymiyya gives two answers: either the opponent did not mean this, or if he did, it is false. Thus he provides three refutations in total.

Key Terms
الأمور الإضافية (al-umūr al-iḍāfiyya)
المتضائفات (al-mutadhā'ifāt)
التغليط (al-taghlīṭ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya identify as the cause of deception in the opponent's statement?
A) Ignorance of logic
B) Equivocation between mental and external existence
C) Denial of divine attributes
D) Misunderstanding of creation

Key Takeaway
Relational matters are concepts in the mind that require two relata, but in external reality a single cause can produce multiple correlative attributes without needing the relata to exist beforehand.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of Causality as an Added Relation
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that the causal relation is ontologically distinct from the essence of the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the ontological distinctness of causality and evaluate its force against the logicians' position.

Core Idea
Causality is not an extra link; it is the essence itself acting.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at the claim that the causal relation (al-‘illiyya) is something added to the essence of the cause. The logicians argue that the essence alone is insufficient, requiring an additional relation. Ibn Taymiyya counters that this leads to absurdity: if the relation is distinct, it would itself need a cause, triggering an infinite regress. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to identify ontological assumptions in Kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's position
What do the logicians claim about causality? They claim it is distinct from the cause's essence because the essence alone does not produce the effect.
The opponent is quoted in the first part of the passage.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma
He presents a dilemma: if causality is not distinct, then the opponent's argument fails; if it is distinct, then it must be a separate entity requiring its own cause.
Look for the phrase 'إن لم تكن مغايرة... وإن قيل: هي مغايرة'
3
Analyse the infinite regress objection
If causality is a distinct real attribute, then we ask: what caused that attribute? This leads to an infinite chain, which is absurd.
Ibn Taymiyya implies that positing an additional relation leads to a regress.
4
Assess the conceptual distinction counter
The opponent may say causality is distinct only in the mind. Ibn Taymiyya responds that non-existent mental distinctions cannot be causes.
He dismisses the claim that it is 'distinct in knowledge, not in reality'.
5
Evaluate the overall refutation
Decide whether the argument holds logically. Is the infinite regress necessary? Does the opponent have a way out?
Consider if the conceptual distinction avoids the regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني عشر أنه قال في جواب السؤال الثاني : لا نسلّم أن العلية مغايرة لحقيقة ذات العلة بطلان قوله بأن العلية لكنا ندعي أن تصور حقيقة العلة مع تصور حقيقة المعلول القريب يوجب العلم بكون مغايرة لذات العلة علة لذلك المعلول». فيقال : هذا كلام مناقض لما تقدم مع تناقضه في نفسه. فإن العلة بموجب العلية إن لم تكن مغايرة لذات العلة بطل قولك: إن ماهية العلة وحدها لا تكفي في حصول العلية لأن العلية أمر إضافي، والأمور الإضافية لا يكفي في حصولها الشيء الواحد». فإنها إذا لم تغايرها لم يكن هناك شيء يكون معلولاً، لا للعلة، ولا لها ولغيرها، بل ولا هناك شيء يتصور إذا تصورت العلة والمعلول. وإن قيل : هي مغايرة في العلم لا في الأعيان فإنها أمر عدمي، قيل: وهذا يبطل قوله «فإن العدم المحض لا ا حصول له فضلاً عن أن يفتقر إلى علة لحصوله، ولكن تصورها في الذهن يتوقف على المضافين. وهذا مما قدمنا أنه حق، وهو مُبطل لما قاله
The twelfth way: He said in answer to the second question: 'We do not grant that causality is distinct from the essence of the cause, as a refutation of his statement that causality is distinct, but we claim that conceiving the essence of the cause together with the essence of the proximate effect necessitates knowledge that it, being distinct from the cause's essence, is a cause for that effect.' So it is said: This is contradictory speech, inconsistent with what preceded and self-contradictory. For if the cause, by virtue of causality, is not distinct from the essence of the cause, your statement 'the essence of the cause alone is insufficient for the occurrence of causality because causality is a relational matter, and relational matters are not produced by a single thing' becomes false. For if it is not distinct from it, then there is nothing that becomes an effect, neither for the cause nor for anything else; indeed there is nothing to conceive when one conceives the cause and the effect. And if it is said: 'It is distinct in knowledge, not in reality, for it is a non-existent matter,' it is replied: This invalidates his statement 'absolute non-existence has no reality, let alone needing a cause for its existence, but its conception in the mind depends on the two correlates.' And this is what we mentioned as true, and it invalidates what he said.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a critical examination of the logicians' view that the causal relation (al-‘illiyya) is something additional to the essence of the cause. Ibn Taymiyya presents an opponent's argument that causality is distinct because the essence alone does not produce the effect. He then refutes this: if causality were identical with the essence, the opponent's argument against the single essence fails; if it is distinct, then there must be an infinite regress of additional relations. He also dismisses the claim that causality is merely a conceptual distinction, because that would make it a non-existent entity, which cannot be a cause. Thus, the causal relation is not an extra ontological reality but a description of the essence in relation to its effect.

Key Terms
علّة
علّية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It leads to an infinite regress of causes.
B) It makes the effect unnecessary.
C) It proves the existence of a creator.
D) It contradicts the concept of a single cause.

Key Takeaway
Causality is not an additional ontological relation; it is the very essence of the cause that gives rise to the effect.

The Relativity of Primary and Theoretical Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya refutes al-Razi's proof and concludes that the primary/theoretical distinction is relative to the knower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of al-Razi's proof and articulate why the distinction between primary and theoretical propositions is relative to the knower.

Core Idea
The same proposition can be self-evident to one person and theoretical to another depending on conception.

Al-Razi argued that to avoid infinite regress, some necessary concomitants must be known without a middle term. Ibn Taymiyya refutes this, showing that the proof only establishes an epistemic distinction, not an ontological one. He insists that what is primary for one may be theoretical for another based on completeness of conception.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct al-Razi's proof
Find the argument that if no self-evident concomitants exist, we face infinite regress or unknowability. Highlight his claim that some attributes are necessary without a middle in reality.
This is the position Ibn Taymiyya will refute.
2
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
He concedes that some things are known without proof, but denies that this requires an order in reality. The 'middle' is simply the proof, so the distinction reduces to 'needs proof' vs 'does not need proof'.
The key move: from ontological to epistemic.
3
Understand the epistemic relativity
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states that a proposition may be badihi for Zayd and nazari for Amr, depending on completeness of conception. This is the core of his critique.
Contrast with the absolute view of earlier logicians.
4
Apply the concept to an example
Consider a simple proposition from fiqh or kalam. See how a more complete conception makes it self-evident, while a deficient conception requires proof.
For instance, the prohibition of khamr is self-evident to one who understands its intoxicating effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل ـ برهان آخر للرازي على هذا التفريق ثم ذكر برهاناً آخر على ما ادعاه من أن ما كان لزومه بغير وسط كان بيناً، فقال: برهان آخر وهو أنا إذا عقلنا ماهية فإنه تبقى بعضُ لوازمها مجهولة ويمكننا : تعرف تلك اللوازم المجهولة فلولا وجود لوازم بيّنة الثبوتِ للشيء وإلا لزم التسلسل، وإما عُدم تعرُّف تلك اللوازم. وكلاهما باطلان . قال : وهذا البرهان كافٍ في إثبات أصل المقصود من أن الصفات اللازمة في نفسها يلزم بعضها بوسط في نفس الأمر، وبعضها بغير وسط . وإنما يدل هذا على أن الإنسان قد يتبين له لزوم بعض اللوازم بلا دليل وبعضُها لا يتبين إلا بدليل . الدليل ما كان وهذا لا ريب فيه لكن الدليل كل ما كان مستلزماً للمدلول لا يختص بما يكون علة للمدلول، ولا بعضُ اللوازم في نفس الأمر علة لبعض، ولا كل ما كان بيناً لزيد يجب أن يكون بيناً لعمرو . الأوليات وغيرها الفرق في فتبين أن الفرق الذي ذكره بين الأوليات» و «المشهورات» من أن «الأولي هو الذي يكون حمله على موضوعه في الوجودين حملاً أولاً، لا ثانياً، غلط لا يستقيم، لا في الوجود الخارجي، فإنه ليس في اللوازم ترتيب حتى يكون بعضُها أولاً وبعضها ثانياً، ولا في الذهني. فإن الوسط إنما هو الدليل، فيعود الفرق إلى أن الأوليات ما لا يفتقر إلى دليل، والنظريات ما يفتقر إلى دليل. وهذا كلام صحيح متفق عليه لا يحتاج إلى ما ذكروه ولكن هذا يوجب كونَ القضية أولية ونظرية هو من الأمور لا يحتاج إلى الإضافية - فقد تكون بديهية لزيد نظرية لعمرو باعتبار تمام التصور. فمتى تصور الشيء تصوراً أتم من تصور غيره تصور من لوازمه ما لم يتصوره ذُو التصور الناقص، فلم يحتج في معرفته بتلك اللوازم إلى وسط واحتاج صاحب التصور الناقص إلى الوسط. وأيضاً فهذا لا يوجب كون المشهورات ليست يقينية، كما سنذكره إن شاء الله وسط إلا ذو التصور الناقص
A section: Another proof by al-Razi on this distinction. Then he mentioned another proof for his claim that what is necessary without a middle is self-evident. He said: Another proof: When we conceive an essence, some of its necessary concomitants remain unknown, and we can learn those unknown concomitants. If there were no evident necessary concomitants of the thing, then an infinite regress would follow, or those concomitants would not be knowable. Both are false. He said: This proof suffices to establish the main objective: that the necessary attributes in themselves are sometimes necessitated with a middle in reality and sometimes without. But this only shows that a person may cognize the necessity of some concomitants without proof, and others only with proof. Proof is what it is, and there is no doubt about this, but proof is anything that implies the conclusion; it is not specific to what is a cause of the conclusion, nor are some necessary concomitants in reality causes of others, nor is everything self-evident to Zayd necessarily self-evident to ‘Amr. The distinction between primary and well-known is thus: the claim that the primary is that whose predication on its subject in both existences is a first predication, not a second, is an error that does not hold, neither in external existence, for there is no ordering among concomitants such that some are first and some second, nor in the mental. For the middle is simply the proof, so the distinction reduces to: primary propositions are those that do not require proof, and theoretical ones require proof. This is correct speech, agreed upon, and they do not need what they mentioned. But this makes the proposition's being primary or theoretical one of the relative matters; it may be self-evident to Zayd and theoretical to ‘Amr, depending on the completeness of conception. Whenever a person conceives a thing more completely than another, he conceives of its necessary concomitants what the deficient conception does not, so he does not need a middle to know those concomitants, while the one with deficient conception needs a middle. Moreover, this does not entail that well-known propositions are not certain, as we will mention, God willing. The middle is only for the deficient conception.
Source pages p. 460

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's proof that there must be some self-evident necessary concomitants to avoid infinite regress, then Ibn Taymiyya refutes it. He argues that the proof only shows that some things are known without proof, but that does not require an ontological ordering; the same proposition can be self-evident to one person and theoretical to another depending on background knowledge. The distinction between primary and theoretical is epistemic, not intrinsic. Ibn Taymiyya also insists that well-known propositions (mashhoorat) can be certain, contrary to logicians' claims. The final lines emphasize that the need for a middle term is due to deficient conception.

Key Terms
بديهي
نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Its subject matter.
B) The completeness of the knower's conception.
C) Whether it is from revelation.
D) The number of terms in the proposition.

Key Takeaway
The primary/theoretical distinction is relative to the completeness of conception, not an intrinsic property of the proposition.

Refuting the Claim of Priority of Essential Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the third distinction on priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's two-pronged refutation of the claim that essential attributes are prior in conception or existence to the described.

Core Idea
Priority in conception depends on the mode of conception, not on the essence's nature.

Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at a third distinction used by logicians to separate essential from accidental attributes: that essential attributes are prior in the mind or in existence. He argues that neither form of priority holds universally. By analyzing his refutation, you will learn how to critically evaluate claims about conceptual priority, a skill essential for distinguishing robust from weak definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Distinction Under Attack
Locate the claim that essential attributes (e.g., 'rational' for human) are prior in conception or existence to the described, while accidental necessary attributes (e.g., 'able to laugh') are posterior.
This is the 'third distinction' referenced earlier in the text.
2
Analyze the Refutation of External Priority
Ibn Taymiyya says attributes subsisting in the described cannot precede it in external existence. The essence is always prior. Ask: why does this refute the logicians' claim?
He appeals to 'clear reason' (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql).
3
Analyze the Refutation of Mental Priority
He introduces two modes of conception: summary (mujmal) and detailed (mufaṣṣal). In a summary conception, one can think of 'human' without any attributes, even essential ones. Thus, essential attributes are not necessarily prior even in the mind.
He gives examples: thinking of human without rationality, or animal without sensation.
4
Extract the General Principle
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that priority in conception varies with the level of detail, not with whether the attribute is essential or accidental. The logicians' distinction fails because it conflates logical order with psychological order.
This undermines the whole framework of 'essential attributes' as a basis for definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق الثالث : التقدم في الذهن أو في الخارج وهو أن الذاتي ما لا يمكن تصور الموصوف إلا بعد تصوره بخلاف اللازم العرضي فإنه متصور بعد تصور الملزوم والذاتي هو المقوم. وهذا الفرق أيضاً فاسد فإن الصفة لا تتقدم على الموصوف في الخارج أصلاً. وأما في الذهن فقد تُتصور الصفة والموصوف جميعاً فلا يتقدم تصور الصفة، وبتقدير التقدم فهذا يختلف باختلاف التصور التام والناقص لا باختلاف اللوازم نفسها . فعلى هذه الفروق الثلاثة أو أحدها يعتمدون حتى الذين صاروا يجعلون المنطق في أول أصول الفقه من المتأخرين هذا عمدتهم، كما يذكر ذلك الآمدي، وابن الحاجب، وغيرهما وكلها باطلة. أما التقدم الخارجي فمن المعلوم بصريح العقل أن الصفة القائمة بالموصوف والعرض القائم بالجوهر لا يُعقل تقدمه عليه بوجه من الوجوه . بل إذا اعتبر تقدم عقلي أو غيره فالذات متقدمة على الصفات . وأما في التصور، فالتصور مراتب متعددة - يكون مجملاً ومفصلاً. فالإنسان قد يَخْطُر له الإنسان ولا يستحضر شيئاً من صفاته، فهذا تَصَوَّره تصوراً مجملاً. وقد يخطر له مع ذلك أنه «ناطق»، كما قد يخطر له مع ذلك أنه «ضاحك». وإذا تَصَوَّر الحيوان قد يخطر له أنه «حساس»، كما قد يخطر له أنه متحرك بالإرادة، وكما يخطر له أنه متألم أو متلذذ وأنه يحب ويبغض. وإذا تصور أن الإنسان حيوان ناطق ولم يتصور الحيوان مفصلاً لم يكن قد تصور الإنسان مفصلاً. فما من صفة
And the third distinction: priority in the mind or in existence, which is that the essential is that the described cannot be conceived except after its conception, unlike the accidental necessary, for it is conceived after conceiving the substrate. And the essential is the constitutive. And this distinction is also corrupt, for the attribute does not precede the described in existence at all. As for the mind, the attribute and the described may both be conceived together, so the conception of the attribute does not precede; and if it were to precede, this varies according to complete and incomplete conception, not according to the necessary attributes themselves. They rely on these three distinctions or one of them, even those among the later ones who placed logic at the beginning of usul al-fiqh—this is their mainstay, as al-Amidi, Ibn al-Hajib, and others mention—and all are false. As for external priority, it is known by clear reason that the attribute subsisting in the described and the accident subsisting in the substance cannot be conceived as preceding it in any way. Rather, if any priority, mental or otherwise, is considered, the essence is prior to the attributes. As for conception, conceptions have multiple levels—being summary and detailed. A person may think of 'human' without bringing to mind any of his attributes; this is a summary conception. He may also think of him as 'rational', just as he may think of him as 'able to laugh'. And if one conceives 'animal', he may think of it as 'sensitive', just as he may think of it as 'moving by will', and as he may think of it as 'feeling pleasure or pain', and as 'loving and hating'. And if one conceives that man is a rational animal but does not conceive 'animal' in detail, he has not conceived 'man' in detail. There is no attribute...
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage refutes the third distinction, which claims that essential attributes are prior in conception or existence to the described, while accidental necessary attributes are posterior. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this distinction is also corrupt. First, in external existence, attributes do not precede the described at all; rather, the essence is always prior. Second, in the mind, conception can be summary or detailed. For example, one can conceive 'human' without any attributes (summary), or conceive it with attributes like 'rational' or 'able to laugh'. The priority of an attribute in conception depends on the level of detail, not on whether it is essential or accidental. Even essential attributes like 'rational' may not be present in a summary conception. Therefore, the distinction fails. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that all three distinctions, relied upon by logicians including those who integrate logic into usul al-fiqh (like al-Amidi and Ibn al-Hajib), are false.

Key Terms
التصور المجمل
التصور المفصل
الذاتي
اللازم العرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is the claim that essential attributes are prior in conception false?
A) Because attributes always precede the essence in conception.
B) Because conception can be summary, where the essence is conceived without any attributes.
C) Because accidental necessary attributes are always prior.
D) Because the mind cannot conceive attributes at all.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the priority distinction by showing that in existence the essence precedes, and in the mind, priority varies with the mode of conception, not with essentiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following claim from a usul text: 'The 'illah must be prior in conception to the hukm, just as the genus is prior to the species.' Using Ibn Taymiyya's refutation, explain why this claim is problematic. Give an example where the 'illah is not conceived prior to the hukm in a summary conception.
?

Ibn Taymiyya's Challenge to the Logicians on Mashhurat
Ibn Taymiyya argues that logicians cannot prove mashhurat are not certain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that mashhurat are epistemically necessary for practical wisdom and identify the logicians' failure to provide evidence for their certainty claim.

Core Idea
Mashhurat are not merely accepted opinions; without them, all practical knowledge collapses.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya launches a devastating critique against the logicians' insistence that mashhurat (widely accepted propositions) cannot produce certainty. He shows that practical wisdom—including ethics and politics—depends entirely on such propositions. If they were not known, then nothing of moral knowledge would be certain. He then turns the tables: the burden of proof is on the logicians to show that mashhurat are not certain, and they have provided no evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational claim
Find the sentence where Ibn Taymiyya asserts that all practical wisdom (al-ḥikma al-ʿamaliyya) is built upon these propositions (mashhurat). This is the starting point of his argument.
Look for the Arabic phrase 'المقصود أن الحكمة العملية كلها مبنية على هذه القضايا'.
2
Understand the consequence
Ibn Taymiyya explains the implication if mashhurat are not known. He says that nothing of practical wisdom, including knowledge of praiseworthy and blameworthy deeds and character, would be known. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The phrase 'فإن لم تكن هذه القضايا معلومة لم يكن شيء من الحكمة العملية معلوماً'.
3
Recognize the dialectical move
Ibn Taymiyya is not trying to prove his own position here; he is demanding proof from the logicians. He explicitly says he is 'demanding from them evidence that these mashhurat are not certain'.
Look for 'وطالبناهم بالدليل على أن هذه المشهورات ليست يقينية'.
4
Assess the strength of the challenge
Why does Ibn Taymiyya call the logicians' stance 'stubbornness' (mukābara)? He points out that their position contradicts what they and other rational people actually say and practice. If the logicians claim mashhurat are not certain, they must provide evidence; otherwise, their claim is baseless.
The phrase 'فهو مكابرة النوع من الكلام' indicates the accusation of stubbornness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود أن الحكمة العملية كلها مبنية على هذه القضايا والنفس لها قوتان : العلمية والعملية، والعمل لا بد أن يكون بعلم. فإن لم تكن هذه القضايا معلومة لم يكن شيء من الحكمة العملية معلوماً، ولا شيء من الأعمال والأخلاق المحمودة والمذمومة معلوماً. وهذا مع ما فيه ما فيه من ظاهرة . أنا هذا في مع المناقضة لما يقولونه هم وغيرهم من العقلاء، فهو مكابرة النوع من الكلام لم نقصد إقامة الدليل على فساد قولهم، بل صوّرناه لتعلم حقيقته، وطالبناهم بالدليل على أن هذه المشهورات ليست يقينية، فإنهم لم يذكروا على ذلك دليلاً أصلا. وسنتكلم على ما توهموه دليلاً من
The intended point is that all practical wisdom is built upon these propositions, and the soul has two powers: the theoretical and the practical, and action must be based on knowledge. If these propositions are not known, then nothing of practical wisdom is known, nor anything of commendable and blameworthy deeds and moral traits known. This, despite what it contains of evident contradiction to what they and other rational people say, is a stubbornness of speech. We did not intend to establish proof of the corruption of their statement, but rather we depicted it so that you know its reality, and we demanded from them evidence that these mashhurat are not certain, for they have not mentioned any evidence for that at all. And we will speak about what they imagined as evidence from the third type.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' position regarding the so-called mashhurat (accepted opinions). The author, Ibn Taymiyya, begins by highlighting the indispensable role of these propositions in practical wisdom (al-ḥikma al-ʿamaliyya). He notes that the soul has two faculties—theoretical and practical—and that action requires knowledge. If the mashhurat were not known, then all practical wisdom, including knowledge of praiseworthy and blameworthy actions and character, would be unknown. This, he says, is an evident contradiction to what the logicians and other sensible people claim. He then clarifies that his aim is not to prove the falsehood of their statement but to expose its true nature and to demand evidence for their assertion that mashhurat are not certain (yaqīniyya). He asserts that they have provided no evidence at all. Finally, he promises to discuss later what they mistakenly consider as evidence from the third type (likely referring to the third category of propositions). This is a classic dialectical move: challenging the opponent to provide justification for a claim that contradicts common sense and established practice.

The Certainty of Mashhurat in Ibn Taymiyya
Author refutes the claim that mashhurat cannot be certain due to variability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that mashhurat are certain despite being habitual, and assess its strength against the logicians' objections.

Core Idea
Mashhurat are not inferior to empirical knowledge; they are equally certain and more universal.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' classification that demotes mashhurat (accepted opinions) below certain knowledge. He argues that a proposition's being primary (awwali) is relative, not absolute, and that mashhurat like 'justice is good' are not only obligatory to accept but also fit for demonstration. In this lesson, we will unpack his critique and see how he rehabilitates the epistemic status of common-sense moral and practical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim being attacked
The logicians assert that mashhurat are not certain because they are based on custom, social utility, or psychological states, rather than on necessary intellectual principles. They treat them as distinct from primary or empirical propositions.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's deconstruction
Ibn Taymiyya first relativizes primariness: what is self-evident for one may be theoretical for another. He then points out that the logicians themselves include mashhurat among the categories of propositions that must be accepted, alongside primary, observed, empirical, etc. He argues that generally accepted mashhurat (e.g., 'justice is good') are actually more widely and firmly held than many empirical or mutawatir propositions.
Note the shift: the logicians' own classification already admits mashhurat as wajib al-qubul (obligatory to accept).
3
Identify his alternative position
Ibn Taymiyya does not propose a new category but reasserts that mashhurat are certain and can serve as material for demonstration (burhan). He insists that the logicians' objection—that mashhurat are habitual—does not negate certainty, because all empirical propositions (mujarrabat) are also habitual and yet are considered certain.
4
Evaluate the critique's internal validity
The critique uses analogical reasoning: if empirical propositions are habitual yet certain, then mashhurat being habitual does not preclude certainty. This argument is logically valid. It also relies on empirical observation that mashhurat are more universally accepted than many empirical claims, which is a contestable but reasonable claim.
Check whether Ibn Taymiyya's argument sufficiently addresses the logicians' deeper concern about the source of certainty (custom vs. rational necessity).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : هي من الواجب قبولها وإن لم تكن أولية تقدم. بل ولا ذلك أمراً لازماً للقضية، بل قد تكون بديهية لزيد ونظرية لعمرو بحسب المتصوّر لتمامه ونقصانه . وهذا مما قد تنازع فيه بعضهم، ويدعون أن كل ما كان أوّليّا لزيد فهو أولي لغيره، كما يدل كلامهم على ذلك وأن الآدميين يشتركون في العلم بكل ما هو أولي لكل شخص منهم، لأنها موجب العقل والعقل مشترك . وهذا القول وإن كنا نبطله ونقول : القضية قد تكون ضرورية لزيد نظرية لعمرو . وكذلك غير ذلك من القضايا قد تكون ، المتواترة لهذا محسوسة لهذا ، والمجرّبة لهذا معلومة بالاستدلال لهذا، ونحو ذلك. وإذا كان كذلك فنحن ليس مقصودنا أن هذه القضايا المشهورة أولية أو ليست هي من الواجب قبولها أولية، ولا أنها أوّلية لجميع الناس أو لبعضهم، بل المقصود أنها من جملة القضايا الواجب قبولها التي يجب التصديقُ بها وتكون مادة للبرهان. فإنهم جعلوا المعتقدات تكن أولية ثلاثة : الواجب قبولها ؛ والمشهورات والوهميات. [٤٣٢] والمقصود هنا أن المشهورات العامة، مثل حسن العدل وقبح الظلم، هي من الواجب قبولها، وإن لم نقل أولية فإن الواجب قبولها قد جعلوها أصنافاً أوليات، ومشاهدات ومجربات، وحَدْسيات ومتواترات وقضايا قياساتها معها . وهذه المشهورات إذا لم تكن أولية لم تكن بدون كثير من المجربات، والحدسيات، ونحو ذلك، فتكون مادة للبرهان اليقيني كالمتواترات والمجربات . فإن المتواترات والمجربات ليست أوليات وهذه المشهورات أبلغ من كثير من المجربات والعلم بها والتصديق بها في نفوس الأمم قاطبة أقوى وأثبت من العلم بكثير من المجربات والمتواترات التي تواترت عند بعض الأمم دون بعض . وبهذا الاعتبار فلم يذكروا حجة على أنها ليست من اليقينيات فإن قولهم موجب كونها عاديات الحكم بها العاداتُ، أو الأحوال النفسانية، أو مصلحة النظام» هذا لا ينافي كونها يقينية، بل هو دليل على ذلك كما سنذكره إن شاء الله تعالى فإن المجربات كلها عاديات، وهي يقينية.
The fourth station - the ninth aspect: They are among those that must be accepted even if they are not primary. That has preceded. Rather, that is not a necessary concomitant of the proposition; indeed, a proposition may be self-evident for Zayd and theoretical for Amr depending on the concept's completeness or deficiency. And this is something about which some have differed, claiming that whatever is primary for Zayd is primary for others, as their statements indicate, and that humans share in knowledge of everything that is primary for each person, because it is a requirement of the intellect and intellect is common. And this statement, even though we refute it and say: the proposition may be necessary for Zayd and theoretical for Amr. Likewise, other propositions may be mutawatir for one, sensed for another, and empirical for one, known by inference for another, and so on. And if that is the case, then our intention is not that these mashhurat are primary or not, nor that they are from the obligatory to accept as primary, nor that they are primary for all people or some; rather, the intention is that they are among the propositions that must be accepted, which must be assented to, and which become material for demonstration. For they made the beliefs—in order to be primary—three: those that must be accepted; the mashhurat; and the wahmiyyat. [432] And the intended point here is that the general mashhurat, such as the goodness of justice and the ugliness of injustice, are among those that must be accepted. Even if we do not say they are primary, for those that must be accepted have been made into categories: primary, observed, empirical, intuitive, mutawatir, and propositions whose syllogisms are with them. And these mashhurat, if they are not primary, are not without many of the empirical and intuitive ones, and so on; thus they become material for certain demonstration like the mutawatirat and the empirical. Indeed, the mutawatirat and the empirical are not primary, and these mashhurat are more effective than many empirical propositions, and knowledge of them and assent to them in the souls of all nations is stronger and more established than knowledge of many empirical and mutawatir propositions that are transmitted among some nations but not others. And from this consideration, they have not mentioned an argument that they are not certain. For their statement that they are due to being habitual—judgment by them is due to customs, or psychological states, or the benefit of order—does not negate their being certain; rather, it is evidence for that, as we will mention, God willing. Indeed, all empirical propositions are habitual, and they are certain.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' classification of propositions, specifically their demotion of mashhurat (accepted opinions) below certain knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya begins by clarifying that a proposition's being primary (awwaliyya) is not an absolute attribute but relative to individuals; what is self-evident for one may be theoretical for another. He then refutes the claim that all humans share the same primary propositions because of a common intellect. He insists that the real issue is not whether mashhurat are primary or not, but that they are among the propositions that must be accepted and can serve as material for demonstration (burhan). He points out that the logicians themselves recognized three categories of necessary beliefs: necessary, mashhurat, and wahmiyyat (imaginary). Furthermore, he argues that general mashhurat (like the goodness of justice) are even more widely and firmly accepted than many empirical (mujarrabat) or mutawatir propositions that are known only among some groups. Therefore, the logicians' objection that mashhurat are based on custom or social utility does not undermine their certainty; in fact, it supports it, since all empirical propositions are also based on habit. This is a powerful argument to rehabilitate the epistemic status of common-sense moral and practical knowledge.

Key Terms
المشهورات
أولية
مجربات

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya defends the certainty of mashhurat by arguing that their habitual nature does not undermine their epistemic status, just as empirical knowledge is both habitual and certain.
