The Innate Moral Sense: Fitrah and Wahm in Ibn Taymiyya
Ibn Taymiyya argues for innate moral perception via wahm and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the innate disposition (fitrah) and the faculty of wahm provide a natural basis for moral discernment, and identify his critique of the opposing claim.

Core Idea
Human souls are naturally disposed to love justice and hate injustice — this is the foundation of moral knowledge.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' assertion that humans, if left solely to their senses, reason, and estimation, cannot judge moral propositions. He argues that both the estimative faculty (al-wahm) and reason are naturally equipped to perceive good and evil. Understanding his argument is essential for grasping the Islamic epistemological stance that morality is accessible through fitrah, even before revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Ibn Taymiyya is refuting the logicians' statement: 'If man were left with his sense, reason, and wahm, he would not judge these matters.' Locate this in the translation and note the extreme position he is attacking.
The logicians imply that natural faculties are insufficient for moral judgment.
2
Analyze the function of wahm
Ibn Taymiyya describes wahm as the faculty that perceives non-sensory meanings like friendship and enmity, even without personal benefit or harm. Observe how he uses examples (wolf and sheep, spouses) to illustrate wahm's role in moral inclinations.
Wahm here is not just animal instinct but a human moral sense.
3
Connect wahm to good and evil
He equates the innate love (from wahm) of justice, truthfulness, and goodness with the meaning of 'hasan' (good), and hatred of injustice and falsehood with 'qabih' (evil). This is parallel to sensory beauty/ugliness.
This establishes an objective reference for moral terms based on innate disposition.
4
Analyze the role of reason
Reason distinguishes benefit from harm. Ibn Taymiyya defines 'hasan' as beneficial and 'qabih' as harmful, showing reason also arrives at moral distinctions.
Reason and wahm work together to provide moral knowledge.
5
Synthesize the argument
The universal human tendency to admire virtuous qualities and despise vices (e.g., being drawn to a just person) serves as empirical proof. This discredits the claim that natural faculties are blind to morality.
The argument is empirical and relies on common observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : كون النفوس مجبولة على استحسان الحسن في الذئب غير محسوس، وإدراك الكبش معنى في النعجة غير محسوس، إدراكاً جزئياً يحكم به كما يحكم الحرُّ بما يشاهده . فعندك قوّةٌ هذا شأنُها . فهذه القوة التي سمّوها الوهم» هي التي يُدرك بها الإنسان صداقة الصديق وعداوة العدو، ويدرك بها كلّ من الزوجين ما في الزوج الآخر من الأمر المحبوب، وبها يميل الإنسانُ إلى غيره وبها يَنْفِر عنه . ولهذا يقولون أكبر حاكم على النفوس الوهم . ومعلوم أن هذه القوة تميل إلى الشخص الذي تعلم أنه عادل صادق محسن، وتنفر عن الشخص الذي تعلم أنه كاذب ظالم مسيء، بل تميل إلى هذا الشخص وإن لم يصل إليها من جهته نفع وضُرّ . والنفوس مجبولة على محبة العدل وأهله وبغض الظلم وأهله. وهذه المحبة التي في الفطرة هو المعني بكونه حسناً، وهذا البغض هو المعني بكونه قبيحاً، كما يقال في الصورة الظاهرة هذا حسن» و «هذا قبيح». فالحُسن الظاهر ما يحسه الحس الظاهر والحُسْن الباطن ما يحسه القلب الباطن. وإذا كانت النفوس مجبولة على محبة هذا وبغض هذا فهذا معنى الحسن والقبح. فكيف يقال: «لو تُرك الإنسان وحسَّه وعقله ووهمه لم يقض بها ونفوس بني آدم مجبولة على استحسان هذا واستقباح هذا ؟ ! وأما العقل فأخص صفات العقل عند الإنسان أن يعلم الإنسانُ ما ينفعه ويفعله، ويعلم ما يضره ويتركه . والمراد بالحَسَن هو النافع، والمراد بالقبيح هو الضار. فكيف يقال إن عقل الإنسان لا يميز بين الحَسَن وبين القبيح؟ وهل أعظم تَفَاضُلِ العقلاء إلا من هذا؟ بل وجنس الناس يميل إلى مَنْ يتصف بالصفات الجميلة، وينفر عمن يتصف بالقبائح. فذاك يميل جنس الإنسان إلى سمع كلامه ورؤيته، وهذا ينفر عن رؤيته وسمع بمعر كلامه .
The Fourth Station - The Ninth Aspect: That souls are naturally disposed to approve of the good. The wolf's perception of meaning in the sheep is imperceptible, and the ram's perception of meaning in the ewe is imperceptible, a particular perception by which it judges just as the free person judges by what he perceives. So you have a faculty whose function is this. This faculty, which they call 'al-wahm', is that by which a person perceives the friendship of a friend and the enmity of an enemy, and by which each of the spouses perceives in the other spouse what is lovable, and by it a person inclines toward another and by it he recoils from him. For this reason they say: 'The greatest ruler over souls is al-wahm.' It is known that this faculty inclines toward a person whom it knows to be just, truthful, and good, and recoils from a person whom it knows to be a liar, unjust, and evil; indeed, it inclines toward this person even if no benefit or harm reaches him from that direction. Souls are naturally disposed to love justice and its people, and to hate injustice and its people. This love that is in the innate disposition is the meaning of its being 'good', and this hatred is the meaning of its being 'evil', just as it is said of the outward form: 'This is beautiful' and 'This is ugly'. So outward beauty is what the outward sense perceives, and inner beauty is what the inner heart perceives. Since souls are naturally disposed to love this and hate this, this is the meaning of good and evil. So how can it be said: 'If man were left with his sense, reason, and wahm, he would not judge these [matters]' while the souls of the children of Adam are naturally disposed to approve of this and disapprove of that? As for reason, the most specific characteristic of reason in man is that man knows what benefits him and does it, and knows what harms him and leaves it. What is meant by 'good' is that which is beneficial, and what is meant by 'evil' is that which is harmful. So how can it be said that man's reason does not distinguish between good and evil? Is there any greater distinction among the rational except this? Rather, the entire human race inclines toward one who is characterized by beautiful qualities, and recoils from one characterized by ugly qualities. That one inclines the human race to hear his speech and see him, while this one causes them to recoil from seeing him and hearing his speech.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' claim that humans, by their natural faculties alone, cannot judge moral propositions. Ibn Taymiyya begins by discussing the faculty of wahm (estimation), which perceives meanings not accessible to the senses, such as the sheep's perception of the wolf's hostility. He asserts that this faculty also enables humans to perceive friendship, enmity, and other relational qualities. The wahm naturally inclines toward the just and truthful and recoils from the unjust and false, even without personal benefit or harm. This shows that souls are innately disposed to love justice and hate injustice. Ibn Taymiyya equates this innate love with the meaning of 'good' and this hatred with 'evil', drawing an analogy to sensory beauty. He then addresses reason, which distinguishes benefit from harm, defining 'good' as beneficial and 'evil' as harmful. He concludes that it is impossible to deny that humans can distinguish good from evil, as this distinction is evident in human behavior: people naturally gravitate toward those with virtuous qualities and avoid those with vices.

Key Terms
الوهم
الحسن
القبيح
الفطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) al-‘aql (reason)
B) al-hiss (sense)
C) al-wahm (estimation)
D) al-fitrah (innate disposition)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that human souls are naturally disposed to love good and hate evil, and that both wahm and reason provide innate moral discernment, refuting the logicians' denial of natural moral knowledge.

Hasan and Qabih as Known by Fitrah
Ibn Taymiyya defines hasan as beneficial and qabih as harmful, asserting they are known by innate disposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's definition of hasan and qabih in terms of fitrah and distinguish his position from conventionalist or purely revelational accounts.

Core Idea
Good is what fits our nature; evil is what hurts it.

Ibn Taymiyya here lays out his fifth critique against the logicians by defining the moral terms 'good' (hasan) and 'evil' (qabih) not as arbitrary conventions but as rooted in the innate human disposition (fitrah). This lesson unpacks how he grounds moral epistemology in natural human inclinations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms being defined
Locate the terms 'hasan' and 'qabih' in the passage. Notice that Ibn Taymiyya explicitly gives their meanings in relation to the soul's responses.
The definitions are not given as genus and differentia but as relational properties.
2
Extract the definitions
Hasan is defined as what is compatible with the soul (yula'im) and beneficial (nafi'); qabih is defined as what harms (yadurr) and hurts (yu'dhi). Write these down.
3
Understand the role of fitrah
Ibn Taymiyya states that people are innately disposed (maftura) to love what is compatible and hate what harms. This makes knowledge of hasan and qabih necessary (daruri) in souls.
Fitrah is the innate disposition that provides immediate, unacquired knowledge.
4
Connect to the mashhurat
The passage concludes that people know the well-known propositions (mashhurat) by their fitrah. Thus, moral principles are not merely conventional but rooted in human nature.
This challenges the logicians' view that such propositions are based on custom or authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس : إن مبادىء هذه القضايا أمر ضروري في النفوس، فإنها مفطورة على حب ما يلائمها وبغض ما يضرها والمراد بالحَسَن ما يلائمها وبالقبيح ما يضرها . وإذا كانت مفطورة على حب هذا وبغض هذا فالمراد بقولنا حَسَن» أنه ملائم نافع، والمراد بقولنا «قبيح» أنه ضارّ مؤذ. وهذا أمر فطري فعلم أن الناس بفطرهم يعلمون هذه القضايا المشهورة بينهم .
The Fifth Type: Indeed, the principles of these propositions are necessary in souls, for they are innately disposed to love what is compatible with them and to hate what harms them. What is meant by 'good' is that which is compatible with them, and by 'evil' that which harms them. Since they are innately disposed to love this and hate this, what is meant by our saying 'good' is that it is compatible and beneficial, and what is meant by our saying 'evil' is that it is harmful and hurtful. This is a matter of innate disposition, so it is known that people, by their innate disposition, know these well-known propositions among them.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is the fifth refutation (النوع الخامس) in Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the logicians. He defines the terms 'good' (hasan) and 'evil' (qabih) in relation to fitrah: good is what the soul loves because it is compatible and beneficial; evil is what the soul hates because it is harmful and hurtful. He states that the principles (mabadi') of moral propositions are necessary in souls (daruri), meaning they are known without acquisition. Since humans are innately disposed (maftura) to love what is compatible and hate what harms, the meanings of good and evil are determined by these innate inclinations. Therefore, people know these well-known propositions (mashhurat) by their fitrah, not merely by convention or tradition.

الفطرة

Ibn Taymiyya's Argument for the Innate Basis of Mashhurat
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the universality of mashhurat implies a common innate basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's conditional argument that the universality of moral propositions implies an innate human basis and evaluate its strength.

Core Idea
Universal moral beliefs prove a shared human nature.

Ibn Taymiyya presents a powerful argument that the cross-cultural universality of certain propositions, especially moral truths, cannot be accidental. He traces their origin to the common fitrah (innate disposition) shared by all humans. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and examines its implications for the foundation of ethical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the 'if...then' statement in the Arabic: 'If these propositions had no principle in human powers, they would not be well-known among all nations.' This sets up the logical structure.
2
Extract the universal observation
Note the fact that these propositions are indeed well-known among all nations. This is the observed phenomenon that needs explanation.
Ibn Taymiyya takes this universality as given; his argument assumes its truth.
3
Connect cause to effect
Apply modus tollens: Since the consequent (not well-known) is false, the antecedent (no principle) must also be false. Therefore, there must be a principle in human powers.
The logical form is: If no principle then not universal; but universal; therefore, there is a principle.
4
Assess the conclusion about lawazim al-insaniyya
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that this principle is from the necessities of humanity (lawazim al-insaniyya), i.e., part of human essence, because nations share nothing else universally.
This step identifies the cause as innate (fitrah) rather than acquired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السادس أن يقال : لو لم يكن لهذه القضايا مبدأ في قُوَى الإنسان لم تشتهر في جميع الأمم، فإن المشهور في جميع الأمم لا بد أن يكون له موجب في الفطرة المشتركة بين جميع الأمم. فعلم أن الموجب لاعتقاد هذه القضايا أمر اشتركت فيه الأمم . وذلك لا يكون إلا من لوازم الإنسانية، فإن الأمم لم تشترك كلها في غير لوازم الإنسانية .
The Sixth Type: It is said: If these propositions had no principle in the powers of man, they would not be well-known among all nations. For what is well-known among all nations must have a cause in the common innate disposition among all nations. So it is known that the cause for believing these propositions is a matter in which nations share. And that can only be from the necessities of humanity, for nations do not all share in anything other than the necessities of humanity.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument (النوع السادس). Ibn Taymiyya uses a conditional argument: if moral propositions had no foundation (mabda') in human faculties, they would not be widespread (mashhura) among all nations. Since they are indeed widespread, there must be a cause (muijib) in the common innate disposition (fitrah mushtaraka) shared by all nations. This cause can only be something necessary to humanity (lawazim al-insaniyya), because nations do not universally share anything else. Thus, the universality of these moral beliefs proves they are rooted in human nature, not in accidental cultural factors.

Key Terms
المشهورات
الفطرة المشتركة
لوازم الإنسانية

Key Takeaway
The universality of moral propositions across all nations points to a shared innate human disposition (fitrah) as their cause, which is one of the necessities of humanity.

Refuting Ibn Sina: Perfect Reason Knows Good and Evil
Ibn Taymiyya refutes Ibn Sina's hypothetical man with perfect reason, claiming he would still know good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to Ibn Sina's claim about perfect reason and identify the rational basis for moral knowledge.

Core Idea
Perfect reason alone suffices for moral knowledge; external training is unnecessary.

Ibn Sina proposed a thought experiment: a person created with perfect reason but no exposure to culture or moral training would not judge anything about his likes. Ibn Taymiyya denies this, arguing that such a person would independently know that justice and truthfulness benefit the soul, while injustice and lying harm it. In this lesson, we dissect this critical exchange and examine the epistemological claim about reason's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's Claim
Locate the earlier scholar's assertion: a person with perfect reason but no adab (external training) and no psychic inclinations would not make moral judgments. This is the target of refutation.
Ibn Sina's claim depends on the idea that moral knowledge requires social or psychological input beyond pure reason.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's Denial
The word 'mamnu'' (ممنوع) signals a direct rejection. Ibn Taymiyya does not accept the premise that perfect reason is insufficient for moral discernment.
This is a common argumentative move in Islamic dialectic: denying the claim outright.
3
Extract the Positive Counter-Claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that perfect reason would know that knowledge, justice, and truthfulness are beneficial and pleasurable, while lying and injustice are harmful and painful. This knowledge comes from reason itself, not from external instruction.
The argument is based on intrinsic perceptions of benefit (manfa'a) and harm (ḍarar) that reason can grasp.
4
Assess the Basis of the Argument
Consider why Ibn Taymiyya believes reason alone can know moral truths. He grounds it in the nature of reason (al-'aql) to perceive the good and bad of actions independently, without needing revelation or custom.
This aligns with certain Mu'tazili positions but is consistent with Ibn Taymiyya's broader rationalist tendencies.
5
Note the Incomplete Passage
The source text cuts off mid-sentence, but the direction is clear: Ibn Taymiyya is elaborating on how reason knows these moral truths, possibly using further arguments about the self-evident nature of such judgments.
Despite the cut-off, the core refutation is complete enough for analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السابع : قوله : «لو توهّم الإنسان بنفسه أنه خُلق دفعة تام العقل ولم يسمع أدباً ولم يُطع انفعالاً نفسانياً ولا خُلُقاً لم يقض في أمثاله بشيء». هذا ممنوع . بل إذا كان تام العقل علم أن العلم والعدل والصدق ينفعه وتصلح به نفسه وتلتذ، وأن الكذب والظلم يضره ويُفْسِد نفسه ويؤلمها، ولو قُدّر أنه لا يُعْلِمُ به أحد غير علم العقل بأنه إذا
The Seventh Type: His saying: 'If a man supposed to himself that he was created all at once with perfect reason, and had heard no adab, and had obeyed no psychic inclination nor disposition, he would not judge anything about his likes.' This is denied. Rather, if he were of perfect reason, he would know that knowledge, justice, and truthfulness benefit him and improve his soul and delight it, and that lying and injustice harm him, corrupt his soul, and pain it, even if it were supposed that no one informs him except the knowledge of reason that when...
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is the seventh argument (النوع السابع), directly refuting Ibn Sina's statement. Ibn Sina had claimed that a person created with perfect reason but no exposure to culture, psychic inclinations, or character would not make any moral judgments. Ibn Taymiyya denies this (mamnu'). He asserts that a person of perfect reason would independently know that knowledge, justice, and truthfulness are beneficial and pleasurable to the soul, while lying and injustice are harmful and painful. This knowledge comes from reason itself, not from external instruction. The passage is cut off, but the argument is clear: perfect reason alone is sufficient to discern moral truths, contrary to Ibn Sina's skepticism.

Key Terms
العقل التام (al-'aql al-tamm): Perfect reason, which includes the ability to discern benefit and harm and to make moral judgments. [Note: No significant shift but contrasts with standard philosophical usage where theoretical reason alone does not yield moral knowledge.]
الأدب (al-adab): Cultural refinement or moral training external to reason. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Takeaway

Inner Pleasures as Evidence for Moral Perception
Ibn Taymiyya uses Ibn Sina's discussion of pleasure to refute the denial of moral intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the anti-moral-intuitionist position using the opponent's own admission about inner pleasures.

Core Idea
Inner pleasures prove that moral perception is real, not an illusion.

In this passage from *Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin*, Ibn Taymiyya turns the tables on those who deny that good and evil are perceived by any human faculty. He uses Ibn Sina's own description of inner pleasures—domination, honor, altruism—to show that even the opponent admits these pleasures exist and override sensory ones. The argument is not just philosophical but empirical, drawing on examples from humans and animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent (likely a philosopher) claims that the approval of good and disapproval of evil is not perceived by sense, intellect, or imagination. Locate this claim in the passage: it is the position being refuted at the end, where Ibn Taymiyya says 'your statement that...'
This is the target of the critique.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's initial rebuttal
Ibn Taymiyya addresses the objection that people sometimes take pleasure in what they know is ugly (e.g., unjust gain). He distinguishes bodily pleasure from heart/intellect pleasure, and notes that failure to feel pain from the consequence is due to temporary absence of reason (like a drunkard).
This sets up the distinction between sensory and inner pleasures.
3
Analyze the quote from Ibn Sina
Ibn Sina himself admitted that inner pleasures (from domination, honor, altruism) are superior to sensory pleasures. His examples include preferring the thrill of winning at chess over food and sex, and noble people preferring charity over personal gratification.
This is the opponent's own authority being used against him.
4
Examine the extension to animals
Ibn Taymiyya adds animal examples: a hunting dog hunts while hungry but gives the prey to its owner; a mother animal risks itself for its young. These show that even non-rational beings experience inner pleasures that override sensory ones.
This strengthens the empirical basis and shows the phenomenon is natural, not just rational.
5
Formulate the final refutation
The opponent admitted that inner pleasures exist and are powerful. But pleasure in doing good and pain in doing evil are precisely inner pleasures. Therefore, the opponent's denial that moral perception is perceived by any faculty contradicts his own admission.
This is a classic argument from internal contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : التذاذ الإنسان بالغلبة والحشمة والإيثار
ظَلَم أبغضه الناس وعادوه وغير علمه بأن الله يعاقبه .
بالقبيح
فإن قيل : الإنسان يلتذ بما يراه قبيحاً كما قد يلتذ بما يأخذه ظلماً فيأكله ويشربه العقل لا يلتذ قيل : وإن التذْ بَدَنُه فإن قلبه وعقله لا يلتذ بذلك، بل يلتذ إذا عَدَل. وإن قدر أنه يلتذ به لذة حاضرة فإنه يتألم لقُبح عاقبته عنده . وإذا لم يتألم فلغيبة عقله عن إدراك المؤلم، كما قد يحصل للسكران وغيره من أمور تؤلمه ولا يتألم بها لغيبة عقله عن إدراكها .
وهذا مما قد ذكره ابن سينا نفسه، فقال في «نمط البهجة والسعادة»:
إنه قد يسبق إلى الأوهام العامية أن اللَّذَّاتِ القوية المستعلية هي الحسّية، وأن بذلك ما عداها لَذَات ضعيفة وكلها خيالات غير حقيقية
وقد يمكن أن يُنَبَّه من جملتهم مَنْ له تمييز ما فيقال له : أليس ألذ ما يصفونه من التذاذ هذا القبيل هو المنكوحات والمطعومات وأمور تجري مجراها؟ وأنتم تعلمون أن الإنسان بالغلبة المتمكن من غلبة ما - ولو في أمر خسيس كالشَّطْرَنج والنَّرْد - قد يعرض له منكوح ومطعوم فيرفضه لما يعتاضه من لذة الغلبة الوهمية. وقد يعرض منكوح ومطعوم [الطالب العفة والرياسة مع صحة جسمه] في صحبة حَشَمه فينفض اليد منهما مراعاة للحشمة، فيكون مراعاةُ الحِشمة آثَرُ والدُّ لا محالة هناك من المنكوح والمطعوم . وإذا عرض للكرام من الناس الالتذاذ بإنعام يصيبون موضِعَه أَثَرُوه على الالتذاذ بمشتهى حيواني متنافس فيه ، وآثروا فيه غيرهم على أنفسهم مسرعين إلى الإنعام به .
وكذلك فإن كبير النفس يستصغر الجوع والعطش عند المحافظة على ماء الوجه. ويستحقر هولَ الموت ومفاجأة العَطَب عندَ مناجزة المبارزين، وربما بالحمد والثناء اقتحم الواحد على عدد دهم ممتطياً ظهر الخطر لما يتوقعه من لذة الحمد ولو بعد الموت كأنَّ تلك تصل إليه وهو ميت .
فقد بان أن اللذات الباطنة مستعلية على اللذات الحسية. وليس ذلك في العاقل فقط، بل وفي العجم من الحيوانات فإن من كلاب الصيد ما يقنص الصيد على الجوع ثم يمسكه على صاحبه، وربما حمله إليه والراضعة من الحيوانات تؤثر ما وَلَدَتْه على نفسها، وربما خاطرَتْ مُحاميةً عليه أعظم من مخاطرتها في حمايتها نفسها .
فإذا كانت اللذات الباطنة أعظم من الظاهرة وإن لم تكن عقلية فما قولك في العقلية؟
فيقال له : هذا كله حجة عليك في قولك إن استحسان الحَسَن واستقباح القبيح لا يدركه الإنسان لا بحسه ولا بعقله ولا بوهمه وأنت قد ذكرت أن الإنسان - بل الحيوان - يلتذ بالحمد والثناء، ويلتذ بالغلبة ويلتذ بالإنعام والإحسان والرحمة، أعظم من التذاذه بالأكل والشرب. ومعلوم أن لذة الأكل والشرب مما يُعلَم
Fourth station - Ninth aspect: The human being's pleasure in domination, honor, and altruism. He wronged, the people hate him and oppose him, and he knows that God will punish him for the ugly deed. If it is said: Man takes pleasure in what he sees as ugly, just as he may take pleasure in what he takes unjustly, eating and drinking it, while the intellect does not delight; it is said: Even if his body takes pleasure, his heart and intellect do not take pleasure in that; rather, they take pleasure when he is just. And if it is supposed that he takes pleasure in it as an immediate pleasure, he is pained by the ugliness of its consequence for him. And if he is not pained, then it is due to the absence of his intellect from perceiving the painful, as may happen to the drunkard and others whose affairs pain them but they are not pained due to the absence of their intellect from perceiving them. This is something that Ibn Sina himself mentioned, when he said in 'The Pattern of Joy and Happiness': It may occur to common fancies that strong and superior pleasures are sensory, and that what is besides them are weak pleasures, all of them unreal fancies. And it is possible to alert from among them one who has some discernment, and say to him: Are not the most pleasurable of what they describe of this kind of pleasure the sexual pleasures and foods and things of that nature? And you know that a man, through domination, who is able to dominate something—even in a base matter like chess and backgammon—may be offered a sexual partner and food and he refuses them for what he gets from the imagined pleasure of domination. And a seeker of chastity and leadership, with a healthy body, may be offered a sexual partner and food in the company of his retinue, and he refrains from them out of regard for honor; thus regard for honor is certainly more preferred there than the sexual partner and food. And when noble people are offered the pleasure of benefitting where they find its place, they prefer it over the pleasure of a desired animalistic competition, and they prefer others over themselves, hastening to bestow it. Likewise, the great-souled person considers hunger and thirst insignificant when preserving his face. He considers the horror of death and the suddenness of destruction trivial when facing dueling opponents; perhaps due to praise and commendation, one individual charges against a number of them, riding on the back of danger, because of what he anticipates of the pleasure of praise even after death, as if that reaches him while he is dead. Thus it has become clear that inner pleasures are superior to sensory pleasures. And that is not only in the rational person, but even in dumb animals: among hunting dogs there is what hunts prey while hungry, then holds it for its owner, and may carry it to him; and among suckling animals, the mother prefers her offspring over herself, and may risk defending it more than she risks defending herself. So if inner pleasures are greater than external pleasures even if they are not rational, what do you say about rational pleasures? It is said to him: All this is an argument against you in your statement that the approval of good and disapproval of evil is not perceived by man neither by his sense nor by his intellect nor by his imagination; and you have mentioned that man—rather, the animal—takes pleasure in praise and commendation, takes pleasure in domination, takes pleasure in beneficence, charity, and mercy, more than his pleasure in eating and drinking. And it is known that the pleasure of eating and drinking is known
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is a critical-dialectical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against those who claim that moral judgment (approval of good, disapproval of evil) is not perceived by sense, intellect, or imagination. The author structures his rebuttal by first presenting an objection from the opponent: that humans sometimes take pleasure in what they acknowledge as ugly (e.g., taking unjustly). He replies that even if the body delights, the heart and intellect do not; if there is no pain from the consequence, it is due to a temporary absence of rational awareness, like a drunkard. Ibn Taymiyya then invokes Ibn Sina's own words from 'The Pattern of Joy and Happiness' to show that even the peripatetic philosopher admitted that inner pleasures (like those from domination, honor, and altruism) are superior to sensory pleasures. Ibn Sina's examples include preferring the imagined pleasure of winning at chess over actual food or sex, and noble people preferring to give charity over personal gratification. Ibn Taymiyya extends this to animals: hunting dogs sacrifice food for the pleasure of serving their master, and mothers risk themselves for their young. The conclusion is that these inner pleasures exist and are powerful. The final move is a direct refutation: the opponent himself admitted that humans—even animals—experience pleasure from praise, domination, and charity more than from eating and drinking. But the pleasure of eating and drinking is sensory and known. Therefore, the opponent's denial that moral approval and disapproval are perceived by sense, intellect, or imagination is self-contradictory, because the same inner pleasures he acknowledges are precisely the ones involved in moral perception.

Key Terms
اللذات الباطنة
اللذات الحسية
الوهم
الإيثار

Inner Pleasures as Evidence for Moral Perception
Argues that internal pleasures prove humans and animals perceive good and evil via inner faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of inner pleasures in human and animal behavior and explain how they demonstrate perception of good and evil.

Core Idea
Inner pleasures, not just sensory ones, prove that we perceive moral qualities.

In this passage, the author uses vivid examples from human and animal life to argue that inner pleasures such as delight in praise or altruism are not only real but often stronger than physical pleasures. This contradicts the claim that moral approval is merely conventional.

Guided Path
1
Examine the human examples
The author describes a noble person tolerating hunger for 'face' and risking death for praise. What inner pleasures are they seeking?
2
Examine the animal examples
Hunting dogs holding prey for owners and mothers defending offspring. What do these show about inner motivation?
3
Compare sensory and inner pleasures
The author states inner pleasures are superior (مستعلية). Note that physical pleasures are known by outward senses; inner pleasures by inner senses.
4
Connect to moral perception
The author argues that these inner pleasures involve perceptions of good (جميل) and evil (قبيح). Thus, good and evil are perceived by inner faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمشتهى حيواني متنافس فيه، وآثروا فيه غيرهم على أنفسهم مسرعين إلى الإنعام به. وكذلك فإن كبير النفس يستصغر الجوع والعطش عند المحافظة على ماء الوجه. ويستحقر هولَ الموت ومفاجأة العَطَب عندَ مناجزة المبارزين، وربما بالحمد والثناء اقتحم الواحد على عدد دهم ممتطياً ظهر الخَطَر لما يتوقعه من لذة الحمد ولو بعد الموت كأنَّ تلك تصل إليه وهو ميت. فقد بان أن اللذات الباطنة مستعلية على اللذات الحسية. وليس ذلك في العاقل فقط، بل وفي العُجم من الحيوانات فإن من كلاب الصيد ما يقنص الصيد على الجوع ثم يمسكه على صاحبه، وربما حمله إليه والراضعة من الحيوانات تؤثر ما وَلَدَتْه على نفسها، وربما خاطرَتْ مُحاميةً عليه أعظم من مخاطرتها في ذات حمايتها نفسها. فإذا كانت اللذات الباطنة أعظم من الظاهرة وإن لم تكن عقلية فما قولك في العقلية؟ فيقال له : هذا كله حجة عليك في قولك إن استحسان الحَسَن واستقباح القبيح لا يدركه الإنسان لا بحسه ولا بعقله ولا بوهمه وأنت قد ذكرت أن الإنسان - بل الحيوان - يلتذ بالحمد والثناء، ويلتذ بالغلبة ويلتذ بالإنعام والإحسان والرحمة، أعظم من التذاذه بالأكل والشرب. ومعلوم أن لذة الأكل والشرب مما يُعلَم بالحس الظاهر، فهذه اللذة الباطنة يعلم بالحس الباطن وبالوهم فكيف تقول إن الحس والوهم والعقل لا يعلم به حُسنُ الحَسَن وقبحُ القبيح؟ وما ذكرته من التذاذ الإنسان بالإيثار وتركه الطعام الشهي مراعاة الحشمة ونحو ذلك إنما هو لكونه يرى ذاك قبيحاً وهذا جميلاً، ويلتذ بفعل الجميل لذة باطنة يحس بها . فكيف يقال إن الحسن والقبح لا ينال بشيء من قوى النفس، وإنما يصدق به لمجرد الشهرة فقط من غير مُوجِب حسّي ولا وهمي ولا عقلي؟
With an animal desire that is competed for, and they preferred others over themselves hastening to bestow it. Likewise, the noble-hearted person belittles hunger and thirst when preserving face. And he scorns the horror of death and sudden calamity at the confrontation of duelists, and perhaps due to praise and commendation, one charges against a dark multitude riding on the back of danger, expecting the pleasure of praise even after death, as if it reaches him dead. So it has become clear that inner pleasures are superior to sensory pleasures. And that is not only in the rational being, but also in the dumb animals, for among hunting dogs are those that hunt prey while hungry and then holds it for its owner, and perhaps carries it to him; and the suckling animals prefer their offspring over themselves, and perhaps they risk defending it more than they risk defending themselves. So if inner pleasures are greater than apparent ones even if not rational, what do you say about rational ones? So it is said to him: All this is evidence against your statement that approval of good and disapproval of evil are not perceived by man neither by his sense, nor his intellect, nor his imagination, while you have mentioned that man—even animals—delight in praise and commendation, delight in victory, delight in bestowing favors, kindness, and mercy, more than their delight in eating and drinking. And it is known that the pleasure of eating and drinking is known by the apparent sense, so this inner pleasure is known by the inner sense and by imagination. How then can you say that sense, imagination, and intellect do not know the good of goodness and the evil of evil? And what you mentioned about man's delight in altruism and leaving tasty food out of regard for decorum and the like is only because he sees that as evil and this as beautiful, and he delights in doing the beautiful with an inner pleasure that he feels. So how can it be said that good and evil are not attained by any of the faculties of the soul, and are only affirmed by mere custom without any sensory, imaginative, or rational cause?
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of an opponent who claims that the approval of good and disapproval of evil are not perceived by any faculty (sense, intellect, or imagination) but are merely conventional. The author begins by describing how noble individuals and even animals exhibit behavior that prioritizes inner pleasures over physical ones: a noble person tolerates hunger and thirst to save face, and risks death for praise. Animals too show altruism, like hunting dogs saving prey for their owners and mothers defending offspring. The author then challenges the opponent: if these inner pleasures are greater than sensory ones even in non-rational animals, then how much greater are rational pleasures? The opponent himself admitted that humans and animals delight in praise, victory, and charity more than in eating. The author argues that since physical pleasure is known by the apparent sense, inner pleasure must be known by inner sense and imagination. Therefore, the opponent's claim that sense, imagination, and reason do not perceive good and evil is self-contradictory. The passage thus uses empirical examples from human and animal behavior to demonstrate that moral qualities are perceived by inner faculties, not arbitrary convention.

لذة
حسن وقبيح
شهرة

B) That inner pleasures can override sensory ones even in non-rational animals.
C) That animals do not experience pleasure.
D) That prey is more delicious when shared.

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that inner pleasures are perceptible and prove that moral qualities are perceived by inner senses.

Refuting the Denial of Moral Perception
Argues that internal pleasures prove humans and animals perceive good and evil via inner faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's refutation of the claim that good and evil are merely conventional.

Core Idea
The opponent's own admission that humans and animals seek praise and altruism contradicts their denial of moral perception.

The passage not only presents evidence but also directly confronts the opponent's inconsistency. The author says: you admit that inner pleasures exist and are known by inner senses, but you deny that moral qualities are perceived. How can that be?

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says good and evil are not perceived by sense, intellect, or imagination; only by mere custom (شهرة).
2
Find the opponent's concession
The opponent admitted that humans and animals delight in praise, victory, charity, more than eating. So they acknowledge inner pleasures.
3
Analyze the contradiction
Since inner pleasures are known by inner sense and imagination, and these pleasures arise from moral actions (e.g., preferring others), moral perception must exist via those faculties.
4
Formulate the refutation
The author concludes that the opponent cannot consistently hold both that inner pleasures exist and that moral qualities are imperceptible.

Dissecting Ibn Sina's Definition of Pleasure and Pain
Ibn Sina's definition of pleasure and pain used to refute reason's inability to judge good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in Ibn Sina's definition of pleasure and explain how it supports the refutation that reason can judge good and evil.

Core Idea
Pleasure is the perception of perfection; good and evil are relative to each faculty.

This passage presents the eighth refutation of the logicians' claim that reason alone cannot judge in commonly accepted matters. The author quotes Ibn Sina's definitions of pleasure and pain, which are perceptions of good and evil. This definition shows that good and evil are perceived by the various faculties, including reason, thus countering the opponent's assertion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definienda
Find the two terms being defined: pleasure (اللذة) and pain (الألم). They are defined together.
2
Extract the genus
Both definitions share the same genus: 'perception and attainment' (إدراك ونيل). This is the broader category.
3
Extract the differentia
For pleasure, the differentia is 'what the perceiver considers perfection and good' (ما عند المدرك كمال وخير). For pain, it is 'deficiency and evil' (نقص وشر).
The differentia is subjective to the perceiver's judgment.
4
Analyze the relativity of good and evil
Note that good and evil vary 'بحسب القياس' (according to measure/relation). Examples are given for appetite, anger, and intellect.
'قياس' here means relation or standard, not syllogism.
5
Connect to the refutation
If pleasure and pain are perceptions of good and evil, then good and evil are perceptible. Reason, being a faculty, can perceive intellectual goods (truth, beauty). Therefore, the opponent's claim that reason cannot judge good and evil fails.
The definition is used as evidence that good and evil are not purely conventional but perceptible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثامن ردّ قولهم : إن العقل بمجرده لا يقضي في المشهورات بشيء النوع الثامن أنه قال : تنبيه : إن اللذة هي إدراك ونيل ما لوصول ما هو عند المُدرِك كمال وخير من حيث هو كذلك ، والألم [ هو ] إدراك ونيل ما لوصول ما هو عند المُدْرِك نقص وشر من حيث هو كذلك. وقد يختلف الخير والشر بحسب القياس . فالشيء الذي هو عند الشهوة خير هو مثل المَطْعَم الملائم والملمس الملائم ، والذي هو عند الغضب خير فهو الغلبة والذي عند العقل فتارةً وباعتبار فالحق وتارةً وباعتبار فالجميل ومن العقليات نيل الشكر ووفور الحمد والمدح والكرامة وبالجملة فإن هِمَم ذَوِي العقول في ذلك مختلفة . وكل خير بالقياس إلى شيء ما فهو الكمال الذي يختص به وينحوه باستعداده
The eighth type: Refutation of their statement that reason alone does not judge anything in commonly accepted matters. The eighth type: he said: A note: pleasure is the perception and attainment of something whose arrival is, in the view of the perceiver, perfection and good insofar as it is so; and pain is the perception and attainment of something whose arrival is, in the view of the perceiver, deficiency and evil insofar as it is so. And good and evil may differ according to measure. So that which is good in relation to appetite is like agreeable food and agreeable touch, and that which is good in relation to anger is victory, and that which is in relation to intellect—sometimes and in consideration it is truth, and sometimes and in consideration it is beauty. And among intellectual matters is the attainment of gratitude and abundance of praise, commendation, and honor. In summary, the aspirations of people of intellect differ in that. And every good in relation to something is the perfection that is specific to it and its predisposition inclines towards it.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth refutation of the logicians' claim that reason alone cannot judge in commonly accepted matters (المشهورات). The author quotes Ibn Sina's definition of pleasure and pain as perceptions of good and evil. According to Ibn Sina, pleasure is the perception or attainment of something that the perceiver considers a perfection and good; pain is the perception of something that is a deficiency and evil. Then he explains that good and evil vary according to the faculty or perspective: for appetite, good is agreeable food or touch; for anger, good is victory; for intellect, good is truth or beauty. Intellectual pleasures include gratitude, praise, and honor. Thus, the quote supports the author's contention that good and evil are perceived by the intellect and other faculties, contradicting the claim that reason alone is insufficient for such judgments. The passage ends by stating that every good relative to something is the perfection specific to it, towards which its predisposition inclines. This definitional move grounds moral perception in the faculties, strengthening the argument against the opponent.

Key Terms
لذة
ألم
كمال
قياس

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's definition of pleasure and pain as perceptions of good and evil demonstrates that good and evil are perceptible by various faculties, including reason, thus undermining the claim that reason cannot judge them.

Ibn Sina's Definition of Pleasure and Types of Good
Ibn Sina defines pleasure and distinguishes types of good according to different faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to dissect Ibn Sina's definition of pleasure (al-ladhdhah) into its genus and differentia and identify how goods vary by faculty.

Core Idea
Pleasure is perception of a perfective good; each faculty has its own good.

Ibn Sina's definition of pleasure goes beyond sensory gratification to include intellectual and spiritual pleasures. This passage is foundational for understanding his epistemology and for later critiques by scholars like Ibn Taymiyya. By parsing the definition, you will see how logical analysis applies to psychological and ethical concepts in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. It is 'al-ladhdhah' (pleasure). Note that Ibn Sina gives a parallel definition for pain.
2
Extract the genus
Pleasure is said to be 'idrak wa nayl' (perception and attainment). This is the broader category: it is a cognitive and acquisitive act.
'Nayl' may also be read as 'mayl' (inclination) in some manuscripts, but here it is attainment.
3
Identify the differentia
The differentia is 'ma huwa 'inda al-mudrik kamal wa khayr' (that which, in relation to the perceiver, is a perfection and a good). This distinguishes pleasure from mere perception.
The differentia is relative ('inda al-mudrik), so pleasure varies by faculty.
4
Analyze the types of good by faculty
Ibn Sina lists goods for appetitive (food, touch), irascible (victory), and intellectual (truth and beauty) faculties. This shows how the same definition applies to different domains.
Intellectual goods include gratitude, praise, honor – demonstrating that pleasure is not limited to the sensory.
5
Understand the two aspects of intellectual good
The intellect considers truth (al-haqq) in one respect and beauty (al-jamil) in another. Both are objects of intellectual pleasure.
This distinction is key for Ibn Sina's aesthetics and theory of happiness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه التاسع : اعتراف ابن سينا كون العقل يحب الحق
النوع الثامن ردّ قولهم : إن العقل بمجرده لا يقضي في المشهورات بشيء
النوع الثامن أنه قال :
تنبيه : إن اللذة هي إدراك ونيل ما لوصول ما هو عند المدرك كمال وخير من حيث هو كذلك، والألم إدراك ونيل ما لوصول ما هو عند المدرك نقص وشر من حيث هو كذلك. وقد يختلف الخير والشر بحسب القياس. فالشيء الذي هو عند الشهوة خير هو مثل المطعم الملائم والملمس الملائم، والذي هو عند الغضب خير فهو الغلبة، والذي عند العقل فتارة وباعتبار فالحق وتارة وباعتبار فالجميل ومن العقليات نيل الشكر ووفور الحمد والمدح والكرامة وبالجملة فإن همم ذوي العقول في ذلك مختلفة. وكل خير بالقياس إلى شيء ما فهو الكمال الذي يختص به وينحوه باستعداده الأول. الأول: وكل لذة فإنها تتعلق بأمرين بكمال خيري، وبإدراك له من حيث هو كذلك.
The Fourth Station - the Ninth Aspect: Ibn Sina's acknowledgment that the intellect loves truth. The Eighth Type: Refutation of their saying that the intellect by itself does not judge in the famous [opinions] at all. The Eighth Type: He said: A note: Pleasure is the perception and attainment of something for the arrival of that which is, in relation to the perceiver, a perfection and a good inasmuch as it is so. And pain is the perception and attainment of something for the arrival of that which is, in relation to the perceiver, a deficiency and an evil inasmuch as it is so. And good and evil may differ according to the standard. So the thing that is good with respect to the appetitive faculty is like suitable food and suitable touch; and that which is good with respect to the irascible faculty is victory; and that which is with respect to the intellect, sometimes and in one respect is truth, and sometimes and in another respect is the beautiful. And from the intellectual goods are attainment of gratitude and abundance of praise and commendation and honor. In sum, the aspirations of those possessing intellect differ in that. And every good relative to something is the perfection that is specific to it and toward which it tends by its primary disposition. First: Every pleasure is related to two things: a perfective good and a perception of it inasmuch as it is such.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's definition of pleasure and his classification of goods according to different faculties. The text opens by stating that pleasure is the perception and attainment of something that is, in relation to the perceiver, a perfection and a good; pain is the opposite. Goods vary according to the faculty: for the appetitive faculty, good is pleasant food and touch; for the irascible, victory; for the intellect, sometimes truth and sometimes beauty. Intellectual goods include gratitude and praise. Every good relative to something is the perfection specific to it. Finally, every pleasure relates to two things: a perfective good and its perception. This sets up Ibn Sina's view that the intellect loves truth and beauty.

Key Terms
اللذة
الحق
الجميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what are the two components of every pleasure?
A) A perfective good and its perception
B) A sensory stimulus and a reaction
C) An appetitive desire and its fulfillment
D) A beautiful form and its apprehension

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina defines pleasure as the perception and attainment of a relative perfective good, which varies by faculty—appetitive, irascible, and intellectual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply Ibn Sina's definition of pleasure to the act of reciting the Qur'an: identify the perfective good attained and the faculty involved. Is it intellectual, appetitive, or irascible?
?
Dissect the definition of 'iman' (faith) as found in a kalam text: what is its genus and differentia? Compare with Ibn Sina's definitional structure.

Ibn Taymiyya's Reclamation of 'Haqq' Against Ibn Sina
Ibn Taymiyya responds to Ibn Sina, asserting that the Quranic term 'haqq' encompasses both truth and beauty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic critique of Ibn Sina's separation of truth and beauty, and cite the hadith he uses to support the Quranic inclusivity of 'haqq'.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya returns to the Quranic 'haqq', which includes both truth and beauty, contra Ibn Sina.

Ibn Taymiyya engages with Ibn Sina's idea that truth belongs to the cognitive power and beauty to the practical power. He affirms the intellectual love for both but challenges the terminological boundary. For Ibn Taymiyya, the revealed language of the Quran and Sunnah already unites them under 'haqq'. This lesson unpacks that critique through prophetic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's distinction
Ibn Sina held that 'truth' (haqq) is loved by the cognitive power and 'beauty' (jamil) by the practical power. He treated them as separate categories.
This is implicit in the passage where Ibn Taymiyya says 'Tafriqahu bayna al-haqq wa al-jamil'.
2
Note Ibn Taymiyya's agreement
Ibn Taymiyya agrees that humans have two powers and that the intellect loves both truth and beauty. He calls this 'correct' (sahih).
He does not reject the psychological observation.
3
Locate his criticism
He says Ibn Sina's distinction is 'according to his convention' (bi hasbi istilahihi), implying it is a technical term not rooted in revelation.
The phrase 'otherwise, the language brought by the Quran...' signals the shift.
4
Examine his prophetic proofs
He cites hadith where 'haqq' is used for both cognitive truths (e.g., the witr prayer being truth) and practical goods (e.g., permissible amusements being from haqq).
Highlight the hadith about archery, horse training, and playing with one's wife.
5
Conclude his claim
Thus, in Quranic usage, 'haqq' covers both theoretical truth and practical goodness/beauty. No separate term is needed.
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that Ibn Sina's terminology is unnecessary for Islamic discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال : هذا تصريح بأن العقل يحب الحق ويلتذ به، ويحب الجميل ويلتذ به، وأن محبة الحمد والشكر والكرم هي من العقليات وهذا صحيح، فإن للإنسان قوتين - قوّةً علمية فهي تحب الحق، وقوّةً عملية فهي تحب الجميل والجميل هو الحسن، والقبيح ضده. وتفريقه بين الحق والجميل هو بحسب اصطلاحه، وإلا فاللغة التي جاء بها القرآن وتكلّم بها الرسول لفظ «الحق» منها يتضمن النوعين. كقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «كل لهو يلهو به الرجل فهو باطل إلا رميه بقوسه وتأديبه فرسه وملاعبته امرأته، فإنهن من الحق» وقوله: «الوتر حق، فمن شاء أوتر بركعة، ومن شاء أوتر بثلاث، ومن أوتر بخمس، أو سبع». ومثل هذا موجود في غير موضع من كلامه. ومن هذا الباب قوله: «أصدق كلمة قالها الشاعر كلمة لبيد ألا كل شيء ما خلا الله باطل».
It is said: This is an explicit statement that the intellect loves truth and takes pleasure in it, and loves the beautiful and takes pleasure in it, and that love of praise and gratitude and generosity is from the intellectual things. And this is correct. Indeed, man has two powers: a cognitive power, so it loves truth, and a practical power, so it loves the beautiful. And the beautiful is the good, and the ugly is its opposite. And his differentiation between truth and the beautiful is according to his convention. Otherwise, the language brought by the Quran and spoken by the Messenger, the word 'truth' from it includes both types. Like his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every amusement that a man engages in is vain except for his shooting with his bow, his training of his horse, and his playing with his wife, for they are from the truth.' And his saying: 'The witr is truth; whoever wishes may pray witr with one rak'ah, and whoever wishes may pray witr with three, and whoever wishes may pray witr with five or seven.' And similar to this exists in many places of his speech. And from this category is his saying: 'The most truthful word that a poet has spoken is the word of Labid: Indeed, everything besides Allah is false.'
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's response to Ibn Sina's distinction between truth (haqq) and beauty (jamil). Ibn Taymiyya agrees that the intellect loves truth and beauty, and that the human being has two powers: the cognitive power loves truth, and the practical power loves beauty (the good or beautiful). However, he criticizes Ibn Sina's terminological distinction, arguing that in the language of the Quran and the Prophet, the word 'haqq' includes both types. He supports this with several hadith: the Prophet said that every amusement is vain except for archery, training one's horse, and playing with one's wife, 'for they are from the haqq'; also, 'The witr is haqq'; and the saying of the Prophet that the truest word spoken by a poet is Labid's statement, 'Everything that is not Allah is false (batil).' Thus, Ibn Taymiyya shows that 'haqq' in the revealed texts covers both truth and goodness, contra Ibn Sina's narrow usage.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Separating Truth and Beauty
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' separation of truth and beauty, citing prophetic hadith and Quranic language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' distinction between truth and beauty and cite the hadith evidence he uses to show that 'haqq' includes both in Islamic usage.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Islamic concept of haqq unites truth and beauty, contrary to the logicians' separation.

The logicians distinguished between truth (cognitive) and beauty (practical). Ibn Taymiyya, citing Quran and hadith, rejects this as a mere terminological distinction and shows that in the language of revelation, 'haqq' encompasses both correct belief and moral goodness. This lesson unpacks his argument and the evidence he brings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
What exactly are the logicians asserting that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking? They separate truth (al-haqq) as a cognitive matter from beauty (al-jamīl) as a practical matter.
Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges their distinction as a technical one, but challenges its universality.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that the logicians' distinction is according to their own terminology, not the language of the Quran and the Prophet. In the Islamic usage, 'haqq' includes both truth and beauty.
This is a linguistic and epistemological objection: the revelation has authority over technical jargon.
3
Extract the hadith evidence
Ibn Taymiyya cites a hadith: 'Every diversion a man engages in is falsehood except his shooting with his bow, training his horse, and playing with his wife, for they are from truth and beauty.' Here, both truth and beauty are attributed to the same actions.
Also the hadith on witr prayer calling it 'truth'.
4
Understand the implication
If 'haqq' in the Islamic lexicon already encompasses both, then the logicians' restriction of 'haqq' to mere correspondence is a deviation from revealed language. This has practical consequences for distinguishing valid acts of worship from false ones.
The worship of false gods is called 'bāṭil' (false) not because it is nonexistent, but because the intended purpose is absent.
5
Evaluate the critique
Does Ibn Taymiyya successfully show that revelation unifies truth and beauty? Consider whether his evidence from hadith is sufficient to refute the logicians' terminology or if they could still maintain a secondary technical usage.
This encourages critical thinking about the relationship between revealed language and technical philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال : هذا تصريح بأن العقل يحب الحق ويلتذ به، ويحب الجميل ويلتذ به، وأن محبة الحمد والشكر والكرم هي من العقليات وهذا صحيح، فإن للإنسان قوتين - قوّةً علمية فهي تحب الحق، وقوّةً عملية فهي تحب الجميل والجميل هو الحسن، والقبيح ضده. وتفريقه بين الحق والجميل هو بحسب اصطلاحه، وإلا فاللغة التي جاء بها القرآن وتكلّم بها الرسولُ لفظ «الحق» منها يتضمن النوعين . كقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «كل والحديث لهو يلهو به الرجل فهو باطل إلا رميه بقوسه وتأديبه فرسه وملاعبته امرأته، فإنهن من هو الجميل الحق»، وقوله : «الوثر حق، فمن شاء أوتر بركعة، ومن شاء أوتر بثلاث، ومن أوتر بخمس، أو سبع ). ومثل هذا موجود في غير موضع من كلامه. ومن هذا الباب قوله: أصدقُ كلمة قالها الشاعر كلمة لبيد ألا ! كل شيء ما خَلاَ الله باطِلُ. ومعلوم أن ما عُبد من دونه موجود مخلوق، ولكن عبادته باطلة وضلال، فإنه ليس مستحقاً لأن يُعبَد . فليست عبادته حقاً بل باطلاً، وهو باطل، لأن المقصود منه بالعبادة معدوم.
So it is said: This is a declaration that the intellect loves truth and delights in it, and loves beauty and delights in it, and that love of praise, gratitude, and generosity are among rational matters, and this is correct. For man has two powers: a cognitive power that loves truth, and a practical power that loves beauty, and beauty is goodness, and ugliness is its opposite. Their distinction between truth and beauty is according to their terminology; otherwise, the language with which the Quran came and with which the Messenger spoke, the word 'truth' (al-haqq) encompasses both types. As he (peace be upon him) said: 'Every diversion a man engages in is falsehood except his shooting with his bow, training his horse, and playing with his wife, for they are from truth and beauty.' And his saying: 'Witr is truth, so whoever wishes may pray witr with one rak'ah, whoever wishes with three, whoever wishes with five, or seven.' And similar examples exist in many places of his speech. And from this category is his saying: 'The truest word a poet ever spoke is the word of Labid: Indeed, everything besides Allah is false.' And it is known that what is worshipped besides Him exists as a created thing, but worshipping it is falsehood and misguidance, for it is not deserving of worship. So its worship is not truth but falsehood, and it is false, because the intended purpose of worship is absent from it.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' distinction between truth (haqq) and beauty (jamīl). He begins by acknowledging that the logicians' statement that the intellect loves truth and delights in it, and loves beauty and delights in it, is correct in itself. He explains this by noting that humans have two powers: a cognitive power (quwwa ‘ilmiyya) that loves truth, and a practical power (quwwa ‘amaliyya) that loves beauty, beauty being goodness (ḥasan) and ugliness its opposite. However, Ibn Taymiyya then critiques the logicians' separation of truth and beauty as a matter of their own technical terminology. He argues that in the language of the Quran and the Prophet, the word 'haqq' encompasses both types—both truth (as correctness of belief) and beauty (as goodness of action). To support this, he cites several hadith. The first: 'Every diversion a man engages in is falsehood except his shooting with his bow, training his horse, and playing with his wife, for they are from truth and beauty.' Here, the Prophet describes these permissible activities as both 'jamīl' (beautiful) and 'haqq' (truth). The second hadith: 'Witr is truth, so whoever wishes may pray witr with one rak'ah...' Here, witr prayer is called 'haqq', meaning it is a legitimate and good act. Ibn Taymiyya then connects this to the statement of Labid: 'Everything besides Allah is false (bāṭil).' He explains that things worshipped besides Allah exist as created entities, but worshipping them is false and misguidance because they do not deserve worship. Thus, such worship is not 'haqq' but 'bāṭil', because the intended purpose (al-maqṣūd) of worship is absent from them—they are not worthy. This shows that 'haqq' in the Islamic usage includes both correct belief and morally good action, rejecting the logicians' restriction of 'haqq' to mere correspondence with reality. The passage is a critique of the logicians' terminology from the authority of revelation.

الجميل
القوّة العلمية
القوّة العملية

D) Because the logicians did not have a concept of beauty.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the Islamic concept of haqq, grounded in revelation, integrates truth and beauty, rejecting the logicians' separation as a technical distinction not reflecting revealed language.

Moral Truths as Part of al-Haqq: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universal moral judgments are part of 'truth' and exposes logicians' contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that universal moral propositions are included in the meaning of 'truth' and identify his critique of the logicians' claim that the intellect cannot judge good and evil.

Core Idea
Moral truths are not subjective; they belong to 'truth' and are recognized by sound fitrah, exposing the logicians' self-contradiction.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' assertion that the intellect alone cannot judge moral good and evil. He shows that even they rely on universal moral propositions like the goodness of justice and the evil of injustice, which they themselves call 'truth'. By analyzing their inconsistency, Ibn Taymiyya grounds morality in existence, perfection, and justice, and argues that sound innate disposition (fitrah) recognizes these truths. This critique is part of his broader attack on the logicians' epistemological claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Locate the statement where Ibn Taymiyya quotes the logicians: 'The intellect by itself does not judge anything in such matters – neither good nor evil.' This is the target of his critique.
2
Find the contradiction in their practice
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya points out that the logicians themselves use moral terms like 'truth' (haqq) and 'justice' ('adl) in everyday speech and in their own disciplines, thereby contradicting their stated principle.
He gives examples from the Prophet's hadith and common usage.
3
Analyze the cause of inconsistency
Ibn Taymiyya explains that the logicians sometimes follow sound fitrah (innate disposition) and speak correctly, and sometimes follow corrupted fitrah corrupted by false beliefs, speaking falsehood.
This is an epistemological explanation based on human nature.
4
Understand the metaphysical grounding
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's argument that good actions involve existence and perfection, while evil actions involve non-existence or deficiency. Justice is defined as equality between similars and differentiation between dissimilars, which is the foundation of all existence.
He illustrates with examples from buildings, clothing, and food.

كون هذه القضايا داخلة في مسمى «الحق» كذلك. وهذا مناقض لقولهم : إن العقل بمجرده لا يقضي في أمثال هذا بشيء – لا بِحُسْن ولا بقبح. وهكذا تناقضوا في نفس الوهميات كما سنذكره إن شاء الله . وسبب ذلك أنهم تارةً يقولون بموجب الفطرة السليمة فيكون كلامهم صحيحاً، وتارة يقولون بمقتضى الفطرة الفاسدة التي قد فَسَدت بالاعتقادات الفاسدة فيقولون باطلاً . وهذه القضايا التي اتفقت الأمم عليها مثل حسن الصدق والعدل وقبح الظلم والكذب داخلة في مسمى «الحق» كما تقدم – في كلام الله ورسوله، وكذلك كلام العقلاء قاطبة – يسمون هذا كله «حقاً». ويقولون لصاحب الدين: «له عليه حق» و «أعطيه حقه». وإذا حكم بينهما بعدل وقسم بإنصاف يقولون هذا حق»، وإن حكم بخلاف ذلك يقولون هذا ظلم وجور». وإذا لم يكن له عنده شيء قالوا : «ما له حق، بل هو يدعي الباطل فيسمون الصدق حقاً) والكذب «باطلاً»، كما في كلام الله ورسوله . قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «إن لصاحب الحق مقالاً» ، وفي لفظ : «إن لصاحب الحق اللسان واليد». وقال لكعب بن مالك لما طلب غَرِيمَه : أي كعب! ضَعِ الشَّطْر» قال «قد فعلت يا رسول الله ! » . قال : قم فاقضه» . وذلك لأن الأمور الحسنة تتضمن أمراً موجوداً ماضياً ومستقبلاً، ففيها وجود وكمال الوجود. والأمور القبيحة تتضمن عدماً ماضياً أو مستقبلاً، ففيها نفي الوجود أو كمال الوجود . فإن كان موجوداً كان العلم بوجوده حقاً مطابقاً له، والأخبار عن وجوده كذلك . وأما الكذب المتضمن نفيه والجهلُ الذي [ هو ] عدم العلم به فهو عدم علم وعدم قول حق، بل الكذب من جنس الجهل المركب وأيضاً، العلم كمال وجود والصدقُ كمال وجود، والجهل والكذب صفة نقص . وكذلك العدل كالتسوية بين المتماثلين والتفصيل بين المختلفين، هو تحقيق مبنى الوجود الأمور على ما عليه وتكميلها. ولهذا مبنى الوجود كله على العدل، حتى في المطاعم والملابس والأبنية ونحو ذلك . فالبيت المبني إن لم تكن حيطانه معتدلة، بل كان بعضها أطول من بعض طولاً فاحشاً، أو كان منحنياً غير مستقيم، فَسَد السقف. وكذلك الثياب إن لم تكن على مقدار لابسيها معادلة لهم وإلا لم ينتفعوا بها. وكذلك ما يصنع من المطاعم والأدوية إن لم تكن أجزاؤه معتدلة في الصفة والقدر – في الكم والكيف – فَسَد وكان مُضِرًا لا نافعاً فهذا موجود في الأمور المحسوسة أن العدل فيها حَسَن، أي تحصل به المنفعة والمصلحة؛ والظلم فيها قبيح، أي تحصل به المضرة والفساد. وعلم الأخلاق والسياسة عندهم وعند سائر العقلاء مبني على العدل. ولهذا جعلوا كمال الإنسان العَمَلي أربعة أمور – إصلاح الشهوة والغضب، والعدل بينهما، وفي العلم بذلك . والذي ذكروه هو بعض صفات الكمال التي أرسل الله بها رُسُله وأنزل بها كتبه والاقتصار على ما ذكروه لا تحصل به السعادة التي هي كمال الإنسان ، ولكنه من الأمور المعتبرة فيها . وقد بسطنا هذه الأمور وبينا قصور فلسفتهم عن حصول السعادة والكمال بها، وأنها أبلغ في القصور من دين اليهود والنصارى بعد النسخ والتبديل، وأن ما عندهم إذا أخرج منه الحق وتُرك الباطل كان جزءاً من الأجزاء المحصلة للسعادة، وفيه أمور كثيرة باطلة وأمور هي حق لكن ليس مما تحصل به السعادة والكمال.
That these propositions are included in the meaning of 'truth' likewise. And this contradicts their saying: 'The intellect by itself does not judge anything in such matters – neither good nor evil.' Thus they contradict themselves in the same conceptual fancies as we will mention, God willing. The reason for that is that sometimes they speak according to sound innate disposition, so their speech is correct, and sometimes they speak according to corrupted innate disposition which has been corrupted by corrupt beliefs, so they speak falsehood. And these propositions upon which the nations have agreed, such as the goodness of truthfulness and justice and the evil of injustice and falsehood, are included in the meaning of 'truth' as has preceded – in the speech of God and His Messenger, and likewise the speech of all intelligent people – they call all of this 'truth'. They say to the owner of a right: 'He has a right upon him' and 'Give him his right.' And when he judges between them with justice and divides with fairness, they say 'this is truth', and if he judges contrary to that they say 'this is injustice and tyranny.' And if he has nothing with him they say: 'He has no right, rather he claims falsehood.' So they call truthfulness 'truth' and falsehood 'falsehood,' as in the speech of God and His Messenger. The Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'Indeed the owner of a right has a say,' and in another wording: 'Indeed the owner of a right has the tongue and the hand.' And he said to Ka'b ibn Malik when he demanded his debtor: 'O Ka'b! Reduce the portion.' He said: 'I have done so, O Messenger of God!' He said: 'Arise and pay him.' That is because good things include an existent matter past and future, so in them is existence and perfection of existence. And ugly things include a non-existence past or future, so in them is negation of existence or negation of perfection of existence. If it is existent, knowledge of its existence is truth corresponding to it, and information about its existence likewise. As for falsehood which includes its negation, and ignorance which is absence of knowledge of it, it is absence of knowledge and absence of truthful speech; rather falsehood is of the kind of compound ignorance. Also, knowledge is perfection of existence and truthfulness is perfection of existence, while ignorance and falsehood are attributes of deficiency. Likewise, justice is equality between similars and differentiation between dissimilars; it is the realization of the foundation of existence of things upon what they are and their completion. Therefore the whole foundation of existence is upon justice, even in foods, clothing, buildings, and the like. So if a building's walls are not balanced, rather some are excessively longer than others, or it is curved not straight, the roof becomes corrupt. Likewise garments, if they are not proportioned to the measure of their wearers, they will not be beneficial. Likewise what is made of foods and medicines, if its parts are not balanced in quality and quantity – in amount and kind – it becomes corrupt and harmful, not beneficial. This is found in sensory things: that justice in them is good, i.e., benefit and interest are attained by it; and injustice in them is ugly, i.e., harm and corruption are attained by it. And the science of ethics and politics according to them and according to all intelligent people is built upon justice. Thus they made the practical perfection of man four things – reforming appetite and anger, justice between them, and knowledge of that. What they mentioned are some of the attributes of perfection which God sent His messengers with and revealed His books upon, and confining to what they mentioned does not attain the happiness which is the perfection of man, but it is among the matters considered therein. And we have detailed these matters and shown the inadequacy of their philosophy in attaining happiness and perfection through it, and that it is more deficient than the religion of the Jews and Christians after abrogation and alteration, and that what they have – if the truth is extracted from it and the falsehood is left – is a part of the parts that achieve happiness, and in it are many false matters and matters that are true but not of what achieves happiness and perfection.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that the intellect alone cannot judge good and evil. He begins by pointing out their self-contradiction: they assert that reason does not pronounce on such matters, yet they themselves rely on universal moral propositions such as the goodness of truthfulness and justice and the evil of injustice and falsehood, which they include under the meaning of 'truth' (al-haqq). Ibn Taymiyya explains that the cause of their inconsistency is that sometimes they follow the sound innate disposition (fitra salima) and speak correctly, and other times they follow a corrupted fitra corrupted by corrupt beliefs and speak falsehood. He then elaborates that these universal moral judgments are recognized by all nations and are part of the meaning of 'truth' as used in the Qur'an and Sunnah and by all rational people. He gives examples from the Prophet's hadith about the owner of a right having a say and the story with Ka'b ibn Malik. He then provides a metaphysical foundation: good actions involve existence and perfection of existence, while evil actions involve non-existence or deficiency. Truthfulness corresponds to reality; falsehood is a form of compound ignorance. Knowledge and truthfulness are perfections, ignorance and falsehood are deficiencies. Next, he defines justice as equality between similars and differentiation between dissimilars, which is the foundation of all existence, illustrated by examples from building, clothing, food, and medicine. He asserts that the sciences of ethics and politics, as understood by all rational people, are built upon justice. The logicians' classification of practical perfection into four virtues (appetite, anger, justice, and knowledge) is acknowledged as part of the truth sent by God, but Ibn Taymiyya criticizes their philosophy for being insufficient for true happiness and perfection, even less than the corrupted religions of Jews and Christians. He concludes that their philosophy contains some truth but much falsehood, and the truth within it does not lead to ultimate happiness.

Key Terms
الحق
العدل
الصدق
الكذب
الفطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the cause of the logicians' inconsistency in moral judgments?
A) They rely on revelation for some judgments and reason for others.
B) They follow sound innate disposition sometimes and corrupted innate disposition at other times.
C) They use different logical methods for different propositions.
D) They lack a systematic philosophy.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universal moral truths are part of 'truth' and are recognized by sound fitrah, exposing the logicians' self-contradiction in claiming reason cannot judge good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
